CIO of Marc & Ben's Multi-Family Office: SpaceX IPO, Anthropic & OpenAI

Sourcery with Molly O'Shea(45.2K)·57:12·4.8K views·2026-05-22
Molly O'SheaHost, Sourcery Michel Del BuonoChief Investment Officer, a16z Perennial
TL;DRFor founders preparing for high-value liquidity events, maximizing returns requires a multidisciplinary approach that balances trust structures, such as "stacking" QSBS tax exemptions, with active tax-loss harvesting, while carefully navigating the…
complex risks of...

TL;DR

For founders preparing for high-value liquidity events, maximizing returns requires a multidisciplinary approach that balances trust structures, such as "stacking" QSBS tax exemptions, with active tax-loss harvesting, while carefully navigating the complex risks of secondary market SPVs and private credit illiquidity.

## Pre-Liquidity Planning & Tax Optimization

  • The Tax Exposure Reality: Founders typically hold stock with a zero-cost basis, meaning 100% of their gains are taxable.
      • Depending on state residency, total long-term capital gains tax can exceed 35% (or 23.8% federally for non-tax states).
      • Funds can only go to three places: family, charity, or the IRS.
  • QSBS & Stacking Exemptions: The Qualified Small Business Stock (QSBS) rules exempt up to $15 million in capital gains from federal taxes.
      • Stacking Strategy: Wealthy individuals often multiply this benefit by setting up multiple distinct trusts (e.g., for children or parents), with each trust potentially claiming its own $15 million exemption if structured correctly.
  • The Silo Problem in Wealth Advisory:
      • Attorneys focus strictly on trust and estate structures (GRATs, SLATs, CRTs), while liquid asset managers focus solely on tax-loss harvesting.
      • Investors often fail to properly trade off these strategies because their advisors operate in separate silos. A unified, multidisciplinary approach is required.
  • Donor-Advised Funds (DAFs): These vehicles allow individuals to detach the timing of tax optimization from philanthropic distribution. Founders can donate appreciated stock to a DAF to claim an immediate tax deduction, deferring the actual distribution decisions until later in life.

## Secondary Market Dynamics & SPV Risks

  • The Rise of Level 1 (L1) & Level 2 (L2) SPVs: High demand for pre-IPO shares (e.g., SpaceX, Anthropic, OpenAI) has created complex, nested Special Purpose Vehicles (SPVs).
      • L1 Structures: Because company bylaws restrict direct stock transfers, employees often transfer stock into a personal entity (e.g., "Molly Corp") and sell shares of that entity to buyers.
      • L2/L3 Structures: Buyers of L1 shares then package and sell shares of their own entity (e.g., "Michel Corp") to third-party investors.
      • Structural Risk: With each nested layer, the end investor moves further from the actual cap table, increasing the risk of contract default or the original owner pledging the underlying shares for personal loans.
  • Aggressive Fee Structures: Many secondary SPVs layer in heavy fees, such as 5% transaction fees and "1 and 10" or "2 and 20" carry arrangements.
      • Unlike traditional private equity, these secondary SPVs rarely feature a hurdle rate (minimum return), meaning the manager collects carry on the very first dollar of gain.

## Portfolio Construction & Private Market Illiquidity

  • Resisting Immediate Diversification Pressure: Wealth managers often push clients to immediately liquidate concentrated positions to earn fees on new stock, bond, or private equity portfolios.
      • Michel Del Buono advises that founders often understand their company's stock better than any outside advisor and should consider holding onto concentrated positions for a period post-liquidity, provided they remain engaged with the business.
  • Private Credit & BDC Gates: Many retail investors are rushing into Business Development Companies (BDCs) and evergreen credit funds without realizing they are "semi-liquid" rather than fully liquid.
      • Most BDCs limit quarterly redemptions to 5% of fund assets and reserve the right to suspend redemptions entirely to protect the fund from fire-selling assets.
  • Institutional Rebalancing: Major endowments (such as Yale) are slightly lowering their venture capital and private market allocations, not due to a lack of faith in the asset class, but because weak distributions over the past several years have left their portfolios over-allocated to illiquid assets.

## Tax-Shielding Real Assets & Luxury Spending

  • Depreciation Credit Shielding: Investing in cash-flow-generating real assets—such as real estate, oil wells, and patent portfolios—allows investors to use depreciation tax credits to offset active income.
      • For passive investors, these tax credits must be structured inside a dedicated corporation to bypass IRS limits on individual passive-activity losses.
  • Step-Up in Basis at Death: Real estate held until death receives a "step-up in basis" to current market value, allowing heirs to sell the asset immediately without paying capital gains taxes on its lifetime appreciation.
  • The Reality of Private Jets: Purchasing a private jet is incredibly capital-intensive due to maintenance, crew, fuel, and landing-cycle overhauls.
      • While jets can be depreciated for tax benefits, the owner must strictly prove the aircraft is utilized for active business purposes.

## Notable Quotes

"There's really only three places your money can go. It's your family, it's charity and it's the IRS. And generally speaking, I don't know anyone who prefers the IRS." — Michel Del Buono
"A lot of the preparation is actually sort of psychology, philosophy, family values... If you don't have those basic things thought out, trying to structure the trust itself is not the difficult bit. It's these things you define inside it that are the difficult bit." — Michel Del Buono
"The problem again with alternative assets is that unlike stocks and bonds, they're very, very difficult to understand... expertise matters, especially in these nichey, strange markets." — Michel Del Buono

Where I find that people have difficulty is when you hire attorneys or CPAs. They're going to tell you everything about these trusts, but they're not going to have the knowledge to compare and contrast that to an investment strategy that generates losses. The real challenge, I think, for people is: "how do I trade these things off?" That's where having a multidisciplinary kind of background is really important, because each of those silos doesn't know enough about the other one to trade them off. That is something that's very much overlooked. Sometimes, just barreling down the "I need a bunch of trusts" path and doing all this stuff is not necessarily better than having a tax-loss generating investment strategy that doesn't require you to do all those trusts. That trade-off is not obvious to figure out and navigate. To put it a little bit more directly, what would happen if you don't do that? How much of your capital would be at risk? Well, if you're a founder of a company, your basis—the price which the IRS uses to assess your stock—is zero. That means 100% of your gain is subject to at least long-term capital gains tax, which, depending on the state you're in, can be upwards of 35%. If you're in a non-tax state, then you just have the federal, which is I think 23.8%. Either way, you're talking about a lot of money. Trust and estate attorneys love to say that there are really only three places your money can go: your family, charity, and the IRS. Generally speaking, I don't know anyone who prefers the IRS. Maybe there are some out there. As a result, everyone is trying to avoid that, and that's where either these investment strategies or these trusts come in. The trusts are really for your family, or there is the charity element, which is kind of a trust in some ways. It is putting money somewhere else that you can't access personally, but it still removes it from your taxable base. What are the types of instruments and tools that you use when you set them up? If you set this up, could you break down: all right, we're in a liquidity event, we're going to put all the money into a trust, so how do we then construct the portfolio and what comes off of there? Yeah. So you are probably going to have several trusts. Again, full disclosure: I'm not an attorney. But you're going to have several trusts that will be dedicated to different members of your family or different entities. Each of those should probably have a different investment strategy. If you're young and you inherit—or your parents set up for you—a trust with a lot of stock in it, you have a very long time horizon because you're young. There, the appropriate investment strategy is different than for an individual who's older and has cash needs to fund their life. It's all about figuring out, for each piece of that mosaic of trusts that you're creating, what is the optimal investment strategy to operate inside of them. You can't just photocopy the same thing for all the different trusts. That's where it gets complicated, and I think that's where people sort of get confused. It's not unusual to see people with many, many trusts, which makes the problem that much more complicated. How do you approach QSBS? QSBS is an incredible benefit for small businesses. For founders who start early in the life of the company, they get $10 million—now actually starting at the beginning of this year, up to $15 million—of proceeds from their stock sale that would not be taxed. It's a huge benefit and a huge motivator for people to actually go into startups. I was recently speaking to some people abroad in Asia who wanted to create an entrepreneurial community, and they said: "What's so special about America?" One of the things, I think, is the tax code, the environment, and the culture of trying to start something, including this QSBS benefit. It's a very important motivator to think that, "Hey, if I work like crazy and dedicate myself to something, I get to keep some of the proceeds in an advantaged way." A lot of these strategies of having multiple trusts are to try to multiply these QSBS benefits across the different trusts. When people talk about stacking them, how does stacking work? That's what stacking means. You can have several trusts for your kids, your parents, whatever. Each of those, depending on how you structure them—which is complicated and probably above my legal knowledge—can get, if it's structured right, its own QSBS exemption. Is there any more to that? I'm just curious. Okay, so coming from someone who doesn't know all the nuances between all of this—like I know I asked you this before—how do you lay it out? I know within all of these structures and entities there are like seven more layers, and more nuances, whether it's strategies, portfolios, or where you domicile it, all those sorts of things. Right. Obviously, if you can multiply your QSBS benefit across several trusts, you're going to get that $10 or $15 million of benefit each time, so it's huge. The economics of that are very compelling and easy to understand. But to your point, this is where it delves into people's families, their personal preferences, and how they operate. Some people don't want to leave everything to their kids, and some people do. That dictates how big those trusts are, what kind of strategies are in them, and how you distribute money out to the recipients. When do you give the money to people—when they're 30, or when they're 40? Do you allow them, if they're younger, to invest in a startup that they're running? All of that becomes really personal. That's why this exercise is hard to generalize; it is effectively custom-made by attorneys. It's a lengthy process because you have to decide all these things. I think where people fall down a lot is when they start realizing they need to make these decisions that have long-ranging implications. They don't really know, or they haven't decided yet: "Do I leave all my wealth to my child, or half my wealth?" They get stuck on that, never progress forward, and then miss the boat on getting the tax benefits. So, I think a lot of the preparation is actually psychology, philosophy, and family values. You have to have all that thought out so that when it comes time to structure things, you already know. For example, you might say, "My niece Molly is going to get X amount when she's 40, and here is going to be the trustee that controls that." If you don't have those basic things thought out, trying to structure the trust itself is not the difficult bit. It's these things you define inside it that are the difficult part. What are the biggest mistakes that people commonly make? The biggest one is they don't think about this, and then at the last minute, they try to build all this. They run into all these roadblocks, which are complicated discussions they have to have as a family to think about these things. They are not ready to do that, they haven't thought about it, and they don't have the time. So then they drop the ball in terms of getting these things done. This sounds really trivial, but at the end of the day, you have to be prepared. Some people are very prepared; they have this family values document they wrote out that they all agreed on about how the inheritance is going to work, who gets what, and what the inheritance is for. That makes life a lot easier. In our last conversation, you mentioned you don't advise—I don't know if I'm allowed to say "advise"—but you've seen and suggest that someone may not want to liquidate their entire position on day one, but instead trickle it out and start putting it in different places. So, if you have a liquidity event coming up, how do you manage it across the next year or two? I think for wealth managers and asset managers, it's very self-serving for them to say, "Hey, you should liquidate everything." Obviously, they can't charge you fees on your concentrated position; it's your stock that you brought to the table. But they will charge you fees if they build you a stock, bond, or private equity portfolio. So, it is self-serving for them to tell you to liquidate everything because you should diversify and get out. To some degree, you probably should diversify for a rainy day, but the degree to which you do that becomes a personal preference. I can't put myself in your shoes. Some people are very risk-averse, so they want to get out of everything as fast as they can. Others really want to ride the wave of that company. History has shown that hanging on to your stock—generally speaking, though there are counterexamples to everything—usually works out well because that stock got you to the liquidity point and will probably continue to do pretty well. If anything, you're the most informed person in the world about that stock. For me to come in and tell you whether you should sell it or not is silly; I don't know more than you do. You're the one who needs to assess whether you truly think that stock will continue to run, and how much you should keep. I'd say the vast majority of people should keep it for a while, at least as long as they're engaged in the company. Eventually, there comes a point where some people disengage from the company. Our last conversation was also anchored on the upcoming SpaceX IPO, which is going to be a very large wealth creation event across California, Texas, or wherever alumni have gone. Another topic that is an undercurrent of a lot of the liquidity over the last year and will continue to be is secondaries—whether it's Anthropic, OpenAI, Anduril, SpaceX, or Stripe. With those in mind, could you talk through the best practices for secondary sales, going through these events, and what to look for and what to avoid? The secondary market is very active right now. Everyone wants to get a piece of the brand-name companies, and so this whole industry has sprung up around that with brokers and friends of founders who happen to have a position. The challenge always with these things is that the companies' bylaws are generally structured so that the employees cannot transfer the stock to outsiders. What ends up happening is the employee creates a company, like Molly Corp, and transfers some of her stock into her own company. The company allows that because it's technically a transfer to herself. Then, she sells shares in that company to others. These buyers don't have a direct claim on the stock; they have a claim on Molly Corp. That's called a level-one or L1 structure. What has happened now is there is so much demand that let's say I bought shares in Molly Corp. I then go create Michel Corp and sell shares in that to other people. That is now a level-two or L2 structure. You see L1, L2, and even L3 nested structures. Each time, you are further and further away from the stock, so there is more risk that you don't get what you thought you would. Who is to say that the original person who started the very first company doesn't borrow money against the shares or sell them outright? There are no real controls on that necessarily. People just say, "I pledge to you that I'll give you the returns when the company IPOs." They are not necessarily saying, "I will hold these stocks in escrow for you." There is a lot of legal complexity, and people are so excited about getting in that they gloss over these details. The other thing, of course, is that people layer in a lot of fees and carry. It's not unusual to see 1-and-10 or 2-and-20 structures, or a 5% transaction fee. Often, it's not done at the valuation of the last round; it's done at a significant markup. It is a very complicated space, and I would really ask the viewers to be careful. It is a very hairy, complicated topic. Why do you think so many people are doing secondaries right before large liquidity events? Wouldn't it logically make sense to hold on to it until after? You mean the person selling? Yeah. I think a lot of people have been working at these companies a very long time and accumulating this equity, and they just want to get some liquidity for their lifestyle. That's one of the drivers. Some have left the company, are working somewhere else, and don't feel as attached to it. There are many personal reasons. Even if it might make mathematical sense to hold on to the stock, you can understand why someone would want to sell beforehand when 100% of their net worth is tied up in it. You mentioned the structure of these SPVs having carry and fees. What is a good structure? What should people try to optimize for, and when are they getting fleeced? Really try to be in a structure where you're only one step removed from the shares. Ideally, you are straight on the cap table, though that is really hard to do. Whenever I advise clients, I try really hard to get them straight on the cap table. If you are going to go through a vehicle, and it's just L1, make sure that you diligence the entity offering the shares. Was it set up by a reputable law firm? Are there safeguards in the documents around whether the shares are going to be placed in escrow, or does the original owner retain the right to pledge them and borrow money against them? Each of these contracts is a custom contract, so you need to get into the details. The legal diligence is actually very hard. A lot of people don't realize, for example, that people take loans out against their private stock. Just because you put it into a vehicle doesn't mean the original owner doesn't have the right to take a loan out against it. You have to think of all these eventualities. In order to think about them, you need to know what options these people have to start with. I'm still discovering things as I look at this stuff daily, so it's tricky. Could you walk through the carry portion? Some people don't understand the differences in carry weight. Let's say you buy the stock at $100, and the carry is 10%. Any increase in the value of the stock above $100, 10% of that gain goes to whoever created the SPV. That's the carry. In fields like private equity, carry usually has a hurdle rate—a minimum return to the investor, maybe 6%, 7%, or 8%—before the SPV owner gets the carry. In these secondary situations, I've never seen that. From the first dollar of gain, they get to keep a portion of the profits. There's nothing wrong with that per se, but you need to be aware of it and bake it into your calculations to understand the net amount you'll receive afterwards. Should they take a 5% fee and 10% carry, or should they take 30% carry? That all depends on how much you think the stock will go up. If you don't think the stock will go up a lot, you can hand out more carry. If you think the stock is going to go up a lot, you don't want to give away as much carry. That becomes subjective, and when we do these analyses, we map out different scenarios of where the stock price might end up to understand that crossover point. It is interesting. We talked about this last time with SPVs, and you mentioned this a bit, but even just doing simple background checks and understanding who is the underlying person behind the vehicle is critical. Make sure that they are credible, and it's not some person who just put together something. There was a tweet not too long ago where someone put together a vehicle, fled the country, and never came back. Oh, really? Yeah, and they just took the money for themselves. But remember, whenever you're doing a private investment, you are excluded from standard regulatory oversight by signing a special contract meant only for qualified purchasers who meet certain income thresholds. Those contracts are custom. It might strike you as unfair or poorly structured, but that is irrelevant because these are private contracts. Whatever you signed and agreed to is what counts. Your notion of what is fair doesn't really matter. You've raised your hand and said, "Look, I am a sophisticated person, I understand what I am doing," so you can't turn around and be upset at the outcome. You have to do your legal and operational diligence. What are you most concerned about in the overall macro? I guess this is a wide-ranging question, but looking at every category—the liquidity in secondaries, impending IPOs, private credit, geopolitical tensions, or AI causing a "SaaS-pocalypse" here and there—what are you most looking out for? I think for every space there are so many arcane, idiosyncratic details to think about. There is potential for making a mistake in any asset class, so it's really about being informed and knowing what you're doing. What worries me is the rush into all these different things by people who I feel sometimes have not done their homework. If you haven't done your homework, you may be unpleasantly surprised by the outcome. Whether it's private credit where people didn't realize that BDCs have gates, I'm surprised to see that because it is very clear in the documents that they will only redeem up to X percent of the fund, and after that, they don't have to. Could you explain that more? With BDCs, there is the concept of an evergreen vehicle. Traditional venture capital or private equity is in a closed-end vehicle, meaning it has a specific start date and end date, and along the way you have no right to redeem. You just get your money back toward the end date. In some evergreen structures—not all, as people often conflate semi-liquid structures with evergreen structures—there is a semi-liquid character. Every quarter, the company running the structure might say, "If up to 5% of total investors redeem, we'll meet that, but if it's more than that, we won't." Even that 5% is subject to their ability to execute. They're not going to jeopardize the fund because 5% of the people want to get out and force a fire sale of assets, hurting everyone else in the fund. So, even the 5% is not always guaranteed. People suddenly started asking for redemptions, things hit the limits, and the managers said, "Well, sorry, we can't." This surprised people, even though it was clearly written in the documents. That's why I worry—people rush into these things without realizing the details. Why did all that happen at once, though? It was a huge headline for a week. I think people started looking under the hood of these things and realizing, "Hey, this is actually more complicated than I thought. Maybe I should get my money out." Now there is a narrative around it that I don't know actually maps to the underlying reality at all. People just want out because they're worried they didn't understand what they were getting into. So, is there an opportunity maybe to even buy these things? That's a good question. You have to look at how much they've sold off and try to understand what's inside of them. Because these are custom pools, every one of them has a different mix of assets inside. You can't make a general statement about BDCs because every single one is going to be different in its underwriting, its loan types, and whether they accept PIK (payment-in-kind) loans. If you've gone in there without understanding the semi-liquid nature of things, you clearly haven't done your homework on the underlying underwriting. Expertise matters, especially in these niche, strange markets. Right. In our last conversation, you mentioned portfolio construction and how you weight credit, venture, real estate, cash, and so on. How has that strategy evolved over time, especially with these super volatile markets? It's interesting to see that over the last several years, there has been more and more exposure to private assets by more and more people. Part of it has to do with the wave of increasing wealth in the country, and part of it is a feeling among retail investors that they're being left out. That pressure is bringing more people to these assets. In general, portfolios have gotten more weighted toward these alternative assets, which is not a bad thing. The problem with alternative assets is that, unlike stocks and bonds, they're very difficult to understand. As with the BDC example, you can't just say, "Well, I want to put some BDCs in there." You need to analyze each one and understand it, and then pick a weighting. BDCs are a type of credit, so they are more risky than Treasuries and less risky than stocks. You put some amount in there, but not a huge amount. How much you allocate depends on optimizations and the goal of your portfolio. Going back to our trust discussion, if it's for a very young person, it's okay to have 80%, 90%, or 100% in alternatives because they don't need the cash immediately. You can let the stuff grow at the highest risk level possible. But if you're running a charity and need to pay out 5% a year, obviously you can't lock everything up in private assets. All this stuff comes back together when you're building a comprehensive plan. It's not just pure math or picking something because it's better than something else. What is the goal of the portfolio, and what does it need to generate over time? That is what comes into play. Don't you find it curious that endowments and institutions are lowering their allocations to the venture asset class? Yale, for example, has been a big supporter of venture capital for a long time, but they are lowering their allocations. Meanwhile, private market demand is off the hook and becoming democratized, with all these different vehicles popping up. For the endowments in particular, they run a very specific strategy with a certain weight for each asset class, and they try to stay on top of those weights, which are determined by long-term expected returns. What happened for them is that they simply got too heavily allocated to private assets. Remember, they have to pay to operate the university, so there comes a point where you start worrying about being overweight in illiquid assets. Many of these private vehicles have not distributed money, which has been the big problem. Historically, you would look at patterns of how long it takes to distribute cash, and in the last few years, those distributions have been pretty weak across all private asset classes, not just venture. If you project that forward, you might start worrying that your portfolio is too illiquid, leading to reduced allocations. Is there anything more we're missing out on regarding taxes? Yeah, I think there is one element which is really important. To me, super interesting things happen when you bring two different disciplines together and examine the multidisciplinary aspects. As I touched on before, you hire attorneys and they're going to talk to you all about trusts. You hire liquid asset managers and all they're going to talk to you about is tax-loss harvesting. Very few people know enough about both to help you trade the two off. We've done some work on that, and it turns out that there are many situations where trusts are not necessarily the right answer. They save you tax, but you can achieve the same or even better outcomes using these tax-loss harvesting strategies. I would caution people to not just jump into one of the silos. Look at all of them, and be sure you're advised properly on all the different components—charity, liquid strategies, and trusts—and trade them all off appropriately. Okay, so what do you do? How do you test a manager on that? You're going to have to find an advisor who knows all these things well enough to be able to trade them off. That's the thing: it is quite rare to find people who have that multidisciplinary background. You look disappointed. I just... it's overwhelming, honestly. It's complicated. I can't imagine coming into this completely blind and trying to drum up an entire life's wealth plan in a couple of months. That's why there are so many people who are unsatisfied with the outcomes they have, but they only realize it years later. Yeah, it's tough. There are residential strategies, but there are also commercial strategies. There are also commercial strategies for where you put your money—like if you want to invest in a small business or own a small business and get dividends off of it. But to go into real estate more, how do you see this shape out between residential, commercial, and those different components? Well, it's very different depending on whether you are taxable or not. If you are non-taxable, you just look at the raw returns. But if you are taxable, real estate and real assets more generally—which are assets that have cash flow like oil wells—have very different dynamics. You can buy an oil well? Oh, yeah. There are many strategies where you can buy the output from oil, you can hedge it, and there are all these very interesting strategies. What all these real assets have in common (real estate, oil wells, patent portfolios, anything that has a cash flow) is that they generally have a depreciation tax credit against them, which allows you to shield your taxes to some degree, provided it is structured properly. That is where the difficulty lies. A lot of the standard funds out there don't do the structuring because most of their clients are not taxable. Here is the real problem: let's say you personally invest in real estate but you're a podcaster. Because real estate is not your principal occupation, you can't necessarily leverage all those depreciation credits yourself. The IRS limits how much you can write off from passive activities. So, you have to structure it properly. You have to create a company that effectively uses all those tax credits inside the entity and distributes something net of that to you. What is the most tax-incentivized active thing to own? If structured properly, you can offset almost all the income from a real estate property. Then, because of something called a step-up in basis at death, if the asset is sold after you pass away, there is no tax owed on it either. Along the way, all the income you get from the asset doesn't pay tax because of the depreciation credit. Normally, if you sell it at the end, you would pay a ton of tax, but if you pass away and hand it to your heirs, they get a new basis—a new valuation for the asset that has gone up—so they can sell it with no tax. However, it is a very long-term and illiquid strategy where you have to hold these things for a long time. In the liquid space, you have all these tax-loss harvesting strategies, levered tax-loss harvesting strategies, and strategies that utilize swaps and other derivatives to generate ordinary income losses. There are so many strategies out there, but you have to understand the details and how to turn the leverage up and down. You have to find the right advisor who can guide you. I know this is a totally personal question, but what percentage of new wealth do you recommend spending? I feel like people will want to just go... Spending? Yeah. To the degree you can avoid spending, you're going to increase your final portfolio value. I can't prescribe a number because everyone has their own preferences. Jesper Brodin – Former CEO of Ingka Group (IKEA) | Investment Conference 2026

Norges Bank Investment Management(118.0K)·13:15·833 views·2026-05-22
Jesper BrodinFormer CEO, Ingka Group (IKEA)
TL;DRBy leveraging its deeply rooted corporate culture and choosing optimism, IKEA successfully navigated the pandemic—scaling its digital operations in weeks rather than years—and decoupled economic growth (+24% revenue) from carbon emissions (-30%…
% absolute carbon reduction)...

TL;DR

By leveraging its deeply rooted corporate culture and choosing optimism, IKEA successfully navigated the pandemic—scaling its digital operations in weeks rather than years—and decoupled economic growth (+24% revenue) from carbon emissions (-30% absolute carbon reduction) through cost-saving, climate-smart innovations.

Leadership and the Power of Corporate Culture

  • Frontline Engagement: IKEA maintains "front weeks" where executives work directly on the store floor to ensure decision-makers remain grounded in the reality of operations and customer needs.
      • Former CEO Jesper Brodin spent his first week as CEO working a dishwashing station to stay connected to frontline coworkers.
  • Decentralized Action During Crisis: A corporate culture that encourages learning from mistakes prevented organizational paralysis during the COVID-19 pandemic.
      • Coworkers globally took simultaneous, localized action to keep the business running, fueling a 24% revenue growth despite global disruption.
  • Cost Consciousness: IKEA’s culture of frugality applies to everyone; Brodin notes that upon reaching his 30-year milestone at the company, his automated work anniversary email offered him just three extra days of vacation.

The Digital Transformation and Pandemic Pivot

  • The Danger of Insularity: Prior to its digital shift, IKEA’s strong historic culture had made it too internally focused.
      • Brodin realized early in his tenure that the company's "front window became smaller and our review mirror became bigger," nearly causing them to miss the online retail revolution.
  • Accelerated Digital Scale-up: At the start of the pandemic, IKEA had only one out of its 40 global markets equipped to handle online shopping.
      • Pressed by lockdowns, the company completed a planned 2.5-year digital transformation in just six weeks, shifting to 100% online operations during peak restrictions.
      • Today, online sales make up approximately 30% of IKEA’s total business.

Decoupling Growth from Carbon Emissions

  • Absolute Footprint Reduction: IKEA achieved a 24% increase in revenue while simultaneously reducing absolute carbon emissions by 30% across the full value chain (upstream and downstream).
  • Targeting Raw Materials: Raw materials represent IKEA's biggest climate and financial challenge, making up approximately 36% of total corporate costs and dominating the carbon footprint.
  • Sustainability as a P&L Driver: Instead of incurring extra costs, climate-smart decisions have served as major financial contributors.
      • Over the last two years, the largest contributors to IKEA's P&L cost reductions have been investments in renewable energy, electrical mobility, material innovation, and circularity.

Strategic Optimism and the Long-Term Horizon

  • Choosing Optimism: IKEA views optimism not as a passive mood, but as a deliberate strategic choice to keep the organization solution-oriented.
      • The executive team operates under a shared agreement to actively "manage problems, but lead from opportunities."
  • Radical Transparency: To address complex criticisms, such as forestry practices, IKEA directly engages critics and activists by bringing them, alongside board members, directly to the source in the field.
  • The 200-Year Horizon: Brodin advises leaders to widen their gaze past short-term geopolitical and economic cycles.
      • When Brodin's colleague asked founder Ingvar Kamprad how long-term the company should think, Kamprad responded, "200 years is good."

Notable Quotes

"We're going to be good at managing problems, but we will lead from opportunities because that's how the world is created." — Jesper Brodin
"In came Ingvar [Kamprad]. He could hardly walk, 91 years old... and then he stopped, and he said 'So, what are you doing?' he asked... and I said to him 'We're trying to solve a problem, Ingvar.' Then, he started crying, and he said 'Oh, how wonderful to have a problem,' and then he left." — Jesper Brodin
"Ah, you should think long-term... 200 years is good." — Ingvar Kamprad

President Trump swears in Kevin Warsh as the new Federal Reserve chair — 5/22/2026

CNBC Television(3.4M)·33:31·16.2K views·2026-05-22
Donald TrumpPresident of the United States Kevin WarshChair, Federal Reserve
TL;DRPresident Donald J.
Trump swore in Kevin Warsh as the new Federal Reserve Chair, urging him to maintain absolute independence and steer the central bank back to its core mission of price stability and full employment. Warsh pledged to modernize the Fed by reforming...

TL;DR

President Donald J. Trump swore in Kevin Warsh as the new Federal Reserve Chair, urging him to maintain absolute independence and steer the central bank back to its core mission of price stability and full employment. Warsh pledged to modernize the Fed by reforming outdated economic models, curtailing "forward guidance," and cultivating a culture of institutional excellence.

The Swearing-In and Executive Independence

  • Oath of Office: Kevin Warsh was officially sworn in as the Chairman of the Federal Reserve Board of Governors by Supreme Court Justice Clarence Thomas in the East Room of the White House.
  • Guaranteed Independence: President Trump explicitly called for the central bank's operational independence, instructing Warsh to prioritize objective decision-making over political influence.
  • Distinguished Audience: The ceremony was attended by a broad coalition of political and business leaders, including House Speaker Mike Johnson, Supreme Court Justice Brett Kavanaugh, Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent, former Vice President Dan Quayle, and former Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice.

Refocusing the Fed's Core Mission

  • The Dual Mandate: Both Trump and Warsh emphasized a strict return to the Fed's fundamental, functional responsibilities: maintaining price stability (combating inflation) and achieving maximum employment.
  • Critique of Mission Creep: President Trump criticized past Fed leadership for "losing its way" by drifting into non-monetary issues, such as climate policy and diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI) initiatives, which he argued contributed to historic inflation.
  • Operational Modernization: Warsh outlined a reform-minded agenda for the central bank, which includes:
      • Transforming and modernizing obsolete data collection methods.
      • Rolling back the institution's heavy reliance on inaccurate economic models.
      • Curtailing the practice of issuing "forward guidance" to market participants.
      • Welcoming robust, collaborative debate among the Board of Governors.

Kevin Warsh’s Background and Qualifications

  • Academic and Professional Pedigree: Warsh holds a public policy degree from Stanford University and a JD from Harvard Law School. He studied under Nobel laureate Milton Friedman and was mentored by former Treasury and State Secretary George Shultz.
  • Historical Experience: At age 35, Warsh served as the youngest-ever Federal Reserve Governor during a time of major economic consequence. For the past 15 years, he has served as a fellow at Stanford's Hoover Institution.
  • Praise for Leadership: Trump praised Warsh's reputation across the political and financial spectrum, noting that his stellar credentials and professional temperament made him the undisputed choice for the chairmanship.

Macroeconomic Outlook and Policy Goals

  • Market Reaction: On the day of the swearing-in, the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged over 600 points, hitting a historic record of 50,702, alongside record highs for the S&P 500.
  • Inward Investment and Manufacturing Boom: Trump highlighted a massive economic transition, noting that $18 trillion has been invested in the U.S. over an 11-month period. This capital is driving major domestic construction in automotive plants, AI infrastructure, and pharmaceutical facilities.
  • Aggressive Deregulation: The administration reported a historic 1-to-129 ratio for cutting old federal regulations relative to introducing new ones, aiming to lower costs and foster rapid private sector growth.
  • Pro-Growth Stance: Trump rejected the notion that economic growth inherently fuels inflation, arguing that expansion and supply-side growth are vital to addressing the national debt.

Today marks a return to an institution that I do, in fact, cherish. My goal now is to create an environment in which the best people can do their life's best work, and to face every challenge in the spirit of common purpose and devotion to the national interest—in a word, to excellence. These duties are now mine, Mr. President, because of the trust you have placed in me. I accept them with gratitude and will strive every day to serve our fellow citizens well. Thank you again, Mr. President.

President Trump Participates in a Swearing-In Ceremony for the Chairman of the Federal Reserve

The White House(3.1M)·32:23·84.1K views·2026-05-22
Donald TrumpPresident of the United States
TL;DRPresident Donald J.
Trump hosted the White House swearing-in ceremony for the new Federal Reserve Chairman, Kevin Warsh, emphasizing the need for absolute Fed independence, a rejection of non-core policy distractions, and a return to the dual mandate of price stability...

TL;DR

President Donald J. Trump hosted the White House swearing-in ceremony for the new Federal Reserve Chairman, Kevin Warsh, emphasizing the need for absolute Fed independence, a rejection of non-core policy distractions, and a return to the dual mandate of price stability and full employment.

Appointment and Background of Fed Chairman Kevin Warsh

  • Distinguished Credentials: President Trump highlighted Warsh’s academic and professional background, calling his credentials "second to none."
      • He earned a public policy degree from Stanford University and a JD from Harvard Law School.
      • He studied under Nobel laureate economist Milton Friedman and was mentored by legendary Secretary of State and Treasury George Shultz.
      • At age 35, Warsh became the youngest-ever Federal Reserve Governor, subsequently spending 15 years as a fellow at Stanford's Hoover Institution.
  • The Swearing-In Ceremony: The oath of office was administered in the White House East Room by Supreme Court Justice Clarence Thomas, with Justice Brett Kavanaugh and other high-ranking officials in attendance.
  • Tribute to Alan Greenspan: In his remarks, Warsh paid tribute to former Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan, who recently marked his 100th birthday, pledging to lead the institution with the same "energy and purpose."

Mandate for Federal Reserve Independence and Reform

  • Strict Institutional Independence: President Trump explicitly called for Warsh to operate completely free from political influence.
      • Trump urged Warsh to make autonomous decisions without seeking approval from the executive branch or outside political actors.
  • Elimination of Policy Drifts: The President critiqued the Federal Reserve's recent trajectory, arguing it had "lost its way" and contributed to historic inflation by drifting into non-core issues like climate policy and Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion (DEI) initiatives.
  • Return to the Dual Mandate: The administration directed the Fed to refocus entirely on its two fundamental, statutory responsibilities:
      • Maintaining price stability and low inflation.
      • Achieving maximum sustainable employment.
  • Modernization and Operational Reform: Warsh outlined key reforms he intends to implement to modernize the central bank, which include:
      • Reforming obsolete data collection methods.
      • Rolling back reliance on outdated or inaccurate economic models.
      • Curtailing the Fed’s practice of issuing forward guidance.

National Economic Trends and Policy Indicators

  • Record Market Performance: Trump reported that on the day of the ceremony, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose over 600 points to reach 50,702, with both the Dow and the S&P 500 setting new record highs.
  • Unprecedented Capital Investment: The President highlighted that $18 trillion has been invested in the United States over the past 11 months, noting that the previous global record was $3 trillion.
  • Aggressive Deregulation: The administration continues to slash federal regulations to stimulate private sector growth.
      • In his first term, Trump maintained a 1-to-12 ratio of adding to cutting regulations; the current pace has accelerated to cutting 129 regulations for every new one introduced.
  • Labor and Manufacturing Growth:
      • Jobless claims recently hit their lowest level since 1969, and 5 million Americans have been lifted off food stamps.
      • Trump emphasized that 100% of net new jobs under his administration have been created in the private sector rather than the government.
      • Driven by targeted tariffs, major auto, semiconductor, and medical manufacturing plants are returning to the U.S., with companies like Eli Lilly and Pfizer investing tens of billions in domestic production facilities.

National Security and Geopolitical Strength

  • Substantial Defense Spending: To ensure long-term stability and military superiority, Trump announced the administration will submit a $1.5 trillion military budget.
  • Foreign Policy and Trade Leverage: The President highlighted how aggressive economic policies, including tariffs, have strengthened the U.S. negotiating position with major foreign powers. He specifically cited intense economic pressure on Venezuela and strict sanctions designed to prevent Iran from acquiring a nuclear weapon.

Notable Quotes

"I want Kevin to be totally independent. I want him to be independent and just do a great job. Don't look at me, don't look at anybody, just do your own thing and do a great job, okay?" — President Donald J. Trump
"We want to stop inflation, but we don't want to stop greatness. And so, that's really a very good thing, a very positive thing, and that's what he's looking to do. He's looking to do positive economic growth." — President Donald J. Trump
"Our mandate at the Fed is to promote price stability and maximum employment. When we pursue those aims with wisdom and clarity, independence and resolve, inflation can be lower, growth stronger, real take-home pay higher, and America can be more prosperous. And no less important, America's place in the world more secure." — Fed Chairman Kevin Warsh

How The Best Companies Defend Against Mediocrity And Rot

Y Combinator(2.3M)·50:05·6.4K views·2026-05-22
Garry TanPresident & CEO, Y Combinator Eric RiesAuthor, 'The Lean Startup'
TL;DR1.
TL;DR Standard corporate governance frameworks prioritize short-term shareholder primacy at the expense of long-term innovation, frequently resulting in founders being ousted and company missions corrupted. To build enduring, century-scale companies, founders must adopt...

1. TL;DR

Standard corporate governance frameworks prioritize short-term shareholder primacy at the expense of long-term innovation, frequently resulting in founders being ousted and company missions corrupted. To build enduring, century-scale companies, founders must adopt "mission-controlled" legal structures—such as Public Benefit Corporations (PBCs) or trust-owned models—that legally shield their core values from speculative financial pressures.

2. ## The Trap of Shareholder Primacy and Founder Oustings

  • The Fallacy of "Best Practices": Modern corporate governance rules often force companies to prioritize quarterly stock gains over long-term product value.
      • A Modern Invention: Shareholder primacy is not a natural law of capitalism; it was popularized in the 1980s by academic and judicial consensus (such as Milton Friedman's doctrines) rather than legislative action.
      • The Delaware Loophole: Standard Delaware C-Corporation charters define a company's purpose using a "blank space" legal clause ("any lawful act or activity"), which courts have routinely interpreted as an absolute legal duty to maximize short-term investor returns.
  • The Jeff Lawson / Twilio Warning: Activists vs. Builders: Founder Jeff Lawson successfully scaled Twilio to $4 billion in revenue, but agreed to a seven-year sunset clause on his founder-control supervoting shares.
      • The 199-Day Window: Just 199 days after his supervoting protections expired, activist investors representing less than 0.5% of shareholders forced him out of the company due to temporary post-pandemic stock volatility.
  • The Myth of "Independent" Directors: Misaligned Incentives: Conventional governance advocates for "independent" directors to balance boards. In practice, because these directors are nominated and recommended by venture capital firms for future board seats, they prioritize investor desires over the founder's long-term vision.

The Costco Origin Story: Placing Customers Over Shareholders

  • The Sol Price Philosophy: Fiduciary to the Customer: Sol Price, the founder of FedMart and pioneer of modern retail, treated his relationship with customers as a legal fiduciary duty, ranking his priorities as: customers first, employees second, and shareholders third.
      • Extreme Transparency: If a competitor offered a lower price on an item, Price famously put up signs in FedMart directing customers to shop at his competitor instead, building immense consumer trust.
  • The FedMart Collapse vs. Costco’s Ascent: The Cost of Activism: Price took FedMart public and later brought in private equity board members to help take it private. Instead, the board locked him out of his office in 1975 to chase higher prices and lower wages.
      • The Counterfactual: Under traditional management, FedMart went bankrupt in just seven years, destroying two decades of building.
      • The Costco Solution: A protesting FedMart executive quit and helped Price launch Price Club, which ultimately merged to become Costco. Costco continues to protect Price's customer-first ethos using a highly defensive "governance fortress" that routinely receives poor ratings from standard governance agencies but delivers legendary market performance.

The "Third Way": Building Mission-Controlled Structures

  • The Ethos and Integrity Formula: Systemic Protection: Rather than relying solely on vulnerable "founder control" (which can suffer from hubris and ends upon the founder's retirement or death), companies should become "mission-controlled."
      • The Formula: $\text{Ethos} + \text{Integrity} = \text{Incorruptible}$. True structural integrity means the mission itself holds legal sovereignty.
  • Public Benefit Corporations (PBCs): An Accessible Shield: Transitioning to a PBC is a straightforward, two-page legal filing in Delaware.
      • Legal Airbag: A PBC allows board directors to reject lucrative buyouts or cost-cutting measures from malicious actors by giving them the legal coverage to prioritize a defined public benefit or mission alongside profit.
  • Industrial Foundations: A Proven Alternative: Common in Europe but underutilized in the US, this structure places the voting control of a for-profit operating business inside a non-profit foundation or perpetual purpose trust.
      • Survival Metrics: Academic data shows that companies structured as industrial foundations are six times more likely to survive to year 50 (a 60% probability of survival compared to just 10% for traditional corporate structures).

High-Stakes Governance: Novo Nordisk and Anthropic

  • The $600 Billion Novo Nordisk Case Study: Protecting R&D: Founded in Denmark in the 1920s to commercialize insulin, Novo Nordisk was structured as a for-profit subsidiary of a non-profit foundation to prevent predatory price-gouging.
      • Blocking the Merger: In the early 2000s, investment bankers and the for-profit board pressured the company to undergo a massive $20 billion merger. The non-profit trustees vetoed the deal because it wasn't necessary for the company's survival.
      • The GLP-1 Payoff: This veto saved a quiet, 11-year-old research program that went on to create GLP-1 agonists (the active compounds in Ozempic and Wegovy), eventually driving Novo Nordisk's market valuation past $600 billion—greater than the entire GDP of Denmark.
  • Anthropic’s Long-Term Benefit Trust: Protecting AI Safety: When founders Dario and Daniela Amodei left OpenAI to launch Anthropic, they designed a "Perpetual Purpose Trust" called the Long-Term Benefit Trust.
      • Decoupling Control: This trust uses a set of independent, non-investor trustees whose sole job is to appoint and dismiss board directors, insulating the safety-focused AI lab from being forced to prioritize profit-maximization at the cost of existential risk.

***

Notable Quotes

"The more successful your organization, the more valuable it is as a target. That's what makes it worth taking over. That's what makes it worth stealing from you, is the fact that it is successful." — Eric Ries
"Every concept, every technique in this book has in common is that someone will try to talk you out of it... The thing I didn't really understand is how much evidence we have that these so-called best practices suck. They're like literally value-destroying." — Eric Ries
"One of the more profound reasons to start a company is actually wanting to create something that outlives you." — Garry Tan
"We're in this era now where we have temporary organizations being led by temporary managers on behalf of temporary investors... Then people say, 'Well, why is trust down?' Well, because we've built an economy that this is how it runs." — Eric Ries
The best way to make money is to create more value than you capture, right? To build something that people want. And yet, we're all supposed to pretend these days that we think all kinds of making money are equally good. There are so many ways of making money in our economy today where you can get rich without creating any value at all. I just think, why don't we just stop pretending that we think that's good? Today we have a very special guest, Eric Ries, author of *The Lean Startup*, which was a New York Times bestseller and a crucial playbook for all founders. Eric has a new book coming out called *Incorruptible: Why Good Companies Go Bad... and How Great Companies Stay Great*, and he's here to dive into some of those core themes with us. Welcome, Eric. Hey, man, good to see you. Always fun to hang out. I mean, *The Lean Startup* taught our whole generation and the new generation how to build. Now you're back with a new book called *Incorruptible*. What made you decide to start it? You know, all my books come from pain. I never ask anybody to do something in a book that I haven't done myself. And, man, we've been around this a long time. How many companies have we seen built and created where the thing that made them special gets lost? How many founders lose control of their company and get kicked out? It doesn't become what they hoped it would be because they don't understand how to protect what they created. Yeah. So, I feel like with *Lean Startup*, we created so many companies worth protecting, but we didn't give them the tools they needed to actually stay in control—to actually protect the trustworthiness of the thing that they made. So, I'm sick of that. Going from zero to one, a lot of people spend a lot of time thinking about zero to one, and I think *Lean Startup* is one of the Bibles for being able to figure that out. Thank you for saying that. There hasn't really been a playbook written until now around how you make it last for a hundred years. Absolutely. And, unfortunately, the way we teach leadership and entrepreneurship today, we tell people, "Don't worry, just be successful. Just get to product-market fit. Success will protect you." We say that once you get successful, then you'll be powerful, and that will give you freedom. What we don't tell you—and what I wish someone had told me—is that the more successful your organization is, the more valuable it is as a target. That's what makes it worth taking over. That's what makes it worth stealing from you: the fact that it is successful. So, I think there's a missing ingredient here that was a big blind spot for me. I didn't see this coming, but I've lived it now for quite a number of years and seen so many companies have it happen to them. In the book, I tell this story about how I was coaching a new founder—I call him "the professor" in the book to protect his privacy. He's a genius, an incredible person, and he's building this really transformative technology on the cutting edge of AI plus bioscience. So, it has a super cool, huge upside, and a hell of a danger. Right? Because you could easily use this technology for something really awful, like bioweapons or pandemics. So, he's trying to recruit talent, and he's having to promise people that this is not going to be used for those things. That is part of why he has this massive talent advantage: people trust him and believe that this technology is going to be used in the right way. But then people started asking these tough questions, like, "But aren't we a for-profit company? What if investors want to do something evil?" And he'd say, "Well, I'll tell them no." They'd ask, "Why will you still be working there if you're telling your investors no?" Right? He didn't know how to answer those questions. When he would meet with investors, all these VCs would just say, "Oh, that's nice, honey. Oh, you're worried about that stuff? Don't worry about it. If you're serious about business, this is not the kind of thing you should be worried about." So, he was feeling trapped. Anyway, when I was talking to him, I happened to be on my way to this event commemorating a founder who had huge success—someone we both know well—who had made unbelievable amounts of money for his investors. At their earliest opportunity, they had betrayed him and kicked him out of his own company. We were doing this event to celebrate him, and people had flown in from all over the country: employees, ex-employees, and about a thousand people in total, including people he had laid off who were coming back at their own expense just to be there for him. So, I was explaining to the professor, "Listen, I can't talk right now because I have to go to this thing." I explained about the founder and the people coming in. He said, "Wow, respect. That's the kind of company I want to build someday." And I was like, "You are not listening to me. He doesn't work there anymore. This isn't a party; it's a wake, right?" And he said, "What? Did he die?" I said, "No, he's still alive. He's at the party." He asked, "Did the company die?" I said, "No, the company is still public, a publicly traded company." He said, "What are you mourning?" And this is the thing: I remember being at that party and asking, "What are we mourning? What exactly is it?" I realized we all had this sense, deep in our hearts, that this is not how it's supposed to be. Something had gone wrong here. We trusted this company, and we trusted this guy. He was the mission guardian, the protector of this really important mission to us. Now that he's gone—I like the new CEO, he's a friend of mine, too—but if he makes a promise investors don't like, then he'll be replaced. So what are his promises worth? We're in this era now where we have temporary organizations being led by temporary managers on behalf of temporary investors. The average holding time of stocks is dramatically down, the lifespan of companies is dramatically down, and the average tenure of executives is dramatically down. Then people say, "Well, why is trust down? How come no one trusts anybody anymore?" Well, it's because we've built an economy that runs this way. I think we, as founders, have the final say. We either have to agree to this system or not, and so we actually are the ones propping it up. We're giving it the fresh meat it needs to survive because the system is so value-destroying. Basically, there are Delaware bylaws, is that right? Basically, if you're a Delaware C-corp, you have to relentlessly pursue profit; otherwise, there are grounds to remove you. That specific principle is exactly how the end of that founder's reign at that particular company happened. I still remember the professor because I was telling him, "You're not listening to me, right? You're not getting it." And he said, "Wait, are you saying that's going to be me someday?" I said, "You're on a one-way ticket to this exact outcome because you've adopted the so-called best practices of corporate governance—of how companies are supposed to be built, run, and structured." One of those practices, of course, is what's called shareholder primacy, right? This is the idea that if you are a Delaware C-corp, the thing you make is not a beautiful, living thing that creates products, delights customers, and does good. No, it's just a financial instrument for investment returns. That's all it is. That is actually a very new idea. I think one of the biggest misconceptions for founders is they assume this is some kind of natural law, or a pillar of capitalism going back to Adam Smith. No, Adam Smith would have been like, "What the F are you guys talking about?" This idea only dates to the 1980s. When the professor was talking to me, he asked, "Wait, so is it possible to build an incorruptible company?" That's kind of how the book got its title. And I said, "Well, it's a good news, bad news kind of thing." Everyone says this is impossible—that this kind of corruption of the mission is natural as you get bigger and scale. But that's not true. There are actually choices we can make as founders, especially choices we make early on, that can change the trajectory of the company so that its longevity can be measured in decades and centuries, not quarters. But the bad news was, "Dude, you've already taken wrong steps in this direction." He already had an incorporated Delaware C-corp. I said, "If I pull your charter right now, it says you have to maximize shareholder value." He said, "No, it doesn't. My guy would have never done that to me. I have a great lawyer." I said, "Why don't you call him and then call me back after this party. I'm going to go to this thing; you call me back tomorrow and tell me what he says." I remember talking to him the next day, and he felt so betrayed. He said, "My lawyer said he was doing me a favor by giving me the best-practice documents. He told me that if the most evil company in the world wants to buy this company, I have to sell it to them. If my employees knew that, they'd all quit." Then he said, "Well, we should probably fix that." I mean, "This is the way we've always done it" is sort of what people are going to tell you. "We've always done it." I say in the book that one thing every concept and technique in this book has in common is that someone will try to talk you out of it.
