I kept a running log of all the BS objections and the weird, passive-aggressive "just asking questions" comments we got from anyone in the ecosystem. I wrote them down and said, "Look, here they are. Here's exactly how to answer them, and here's the evidence." This is the part that really blew my mind. I've been working on this for a long time. I built the Long-Term Stock Exchange, trying to put my money where my mouth is. We have to change this ecosystem so we all make more money; we have to do it. The thing I didn't really understand back then is how much evidence we have that these so-called best practices actually suck. They are literally value-destroying. I mean, let's go into one—the most astonishing one. Our friend Jeff Lawson at Twilio built that company from nothing to $4 billion in actual revenue, with the stock up 390% since the IPO. By all accounts, it was a smash, rip-roaring success. And then his supervoting shares expired, and after 199 days, he was out. Less than half a percent of shareholders did that. How did that happen? What is going on? It's honestly unbelievable to me. Well, let me make the case for why he "needed" to be fired, and then you'll see if this makes sense to you. What happened was, he took the company public. He agreed as part of the IPO prep process, as a lot of founders do, that he would have dual-class control—founder control—and be the mission guardian. The protections would sunset after seven years. When you're taking a company public, seven years sounds like a long time. But in the public market, seven years is barely a beginning; it's a handful of quarters. Anyway, that was the deal; he made the deal. His advisors and everyone told him not to worry about it, that he could always extend it. "It's always too early until it's too late" is kind of the idea from the book. So, seven years came and went. Those seven years happened to include the pandemic years, as you well remember. The run-up in telecom and tech stocks was insane, and then that bubble burst, and the stock came way down. At the time he was fired, the stock was down about 80% from its peak. And everyone was like, "Oh, okay, case closed." But if you measure from the IPO, revenue was up. Did the business go down? Was revenue down? Was there some kind of problem? No. And yet, that was enough for them to fire him. What really pissed me off the most is those 199 days. He had run this company for seven years as a public company, and then his protections expired. He didn't even last one year past their expiration. Even if you think he made mistakes, had he really earned so little grace to run his own company? Does the fact that he made billions for his investors count for nothing? What are we doing? What's strange to me—and in the book, I give case studies going back 200 years because it's not just about Twilio; Twilio is a fine company and they're doing great now—is that we have this urge to fire a founder whenever they make a quote-unquote mistake. People say we have to have accountability for founders. True, accountability is important, but oftentimes firing them is the end of the company. Right. Like when Edwin Land was fired from Polaroid. Today, people are like, "Polaroid? Was that that instant camera company?" No, Polaroid used to be an R&D powerhouse. They had 1,500 research scientists on staff. Steve Jobs loved that company and admired them so much. When Edwin Land was fired, Jobs called it the dumbest thing he'd ever heard. They never invented another thing after they fired the founder. So I just think, even if you agree that a founder made a mistake, we jump to the conclusion that firing them and bringing in some suit—doing the standard "best practice" thing—is somehow going to lead to a positive outcome. So often, it doesn't. Because, unfortunately, we're teaching people that the mission can only be protected by the founder. Then, when you get rid of the founder, everyone says, "Well, I guess we have no mission anymore. We just become extractive and try to make money." What I really think is that we shouldn't just be building investor-controlled companies or founder-controlled companies. There is a third way. Interesting. We can build mission-controlled companies where the mission itself has sovereignty, and the company can then last a lot longer. What does that really look like? I mean, Patagonia famously was one of the first to do this, but for someone watching, they might say, "Well, I know a Delaware C-corp, and I know there are supervoting shares, and I shouldn't let those expire. Maybe that saves people from the Jeff Lawson situation." I mean, that would be better. That'd be better than the sunset, for sure. But there is a lot more to this. Let me tell you a story—the legend of Sol Price. Let's move away from tech for a second, because I think it's helpful to understand the precedents that got us into this situation. Sol is widely considered to be the father of modern retail. To give you a sense of how influential he was, when a guy named Sam Walton was thinking about starting a retailer in Arkansas, he named it Walmart as an intentional tribute to the company Sol created, which was called FedMart. FedMart was the original modern retail company, started in my hometown of San Diego in the 1950s. Sol was a lawyer before he became an entrepreneur. As a lawyer, he had been trained that you have a fiduciary duty to your client—meaning you put your client's interests before yours. So when he became a retailer, he asked himself a simple question: "Who is my client?" So many companies get this wrong. He was just like, "This is very simple. I am a fiduciary to my customer. The customer is the client." So he had this clear fiduciary hierarchy: customer first, employees second, shareholders third. The great Peter Drucker said he got it wrong, that it should have been employees first, customers second, shareholders last. The famous Johnson & Johnson Credo is doctors, patients, and nurses first, employees second, communities third, and shareholders last. Do you notice the pattern? Hmm. It's not because shareholders aren't important, but because everyone who has ever studied this seriously understands that shareholder value is like the exhaust that comes out of the engine. When you take the exhaust pipe, put it in the intake, and make that your explicit goal, you don't stand for anything anymore. Product quality suffers. Whatever you think is important—design, quality, the health of your customers—everything becomes expendable on the altar of shareholder value. No company can really endure with that structure. Or they do, and they look like Philip Morris. Or you wind up being the kind of company your grandkids are embarrassed to be related to. We know a lot of current tech companies are on that trajectory right now, and it's sad. I don't want to name names, but think about companies where having them on your resume used to be a gold star, and now people ask, "Why did you stay there?" Yeah, I think there was a stat in your book that really jumped out at me. It's like $8 billion in profit, but there's $300 billion or $600 billion in costs? It's $600 billion, yeah. $300 billion in direct healthcare costs and $300 billion in lost productivity—and obviously the mortality of people dying and not being able to work. It's grim. When people say Philip Morris is profitable, they have to take an incredibly narrow view of profit. They have about $8 billion a year in net income, but studies show they create $600 billion a year in costs in the US alone that have to be borne by others. Someone calculated recently that the tobacco industry makes $6,000 from every customer who dies. Do you want that to be your future? If you don't get the governance of your startup right, no other decision you make in the long term is going to matter, because you won't be there to make it. If someone says, "I have an idea, let's turn this company into Philip Morris," what are you going to do? Back to Sol Price. FedMart embodied this principle of being a fiduciary to the customer. Think about the way Steve Jobs would obsess about design. Steve Jobs used to get into fights with people about the visual design layout of the cables inside computers, even though he didn't want customers to open the case. His engineers would ask, "What do you care? No one's going to see it." But he'd say, "We're going to see it." This was his principle, not shareholder value. If we stand for this principle, good things will happen to us. How you do anything is how you do everything. Now, Steve Jobs got fired because of this exact thing. So, spoiler alert, so will Sol in a second. Sol built his company on this principle. Being a fiduciary to the customer meant that if you tried to undercut him on price, he would literally put up signs inside his own store saying, "Don't buy this product from me. You can get it cheaper at a competitor," and he would have instructions on where to go. He was just like, "I don't care. My job is to get you the lowest price, even if it's not from me." And people will come to you every single time because they trust you. Well, they trust you. They know if there is ever a cheaper option—can you imagine a modern retailer doing that? All these DTC brands where you have to cross-check prices... why don't you just be my ally, and then I'll trust you? He understood that trust is an asset. Customers would drive miles out of their way to shop at FedMart. It was a huge success. After succeeding as a private company, he took it public, and everyone made a lot of money from the IPO. But as a public company, investors kept pushing him. He felt this gravitational pull: he wanted low prices and high wages, while investors wanted high prices and low wages. So, he was always battling investors. Sol was a really stubborn S.O.B. To try to get out of this situation, he brought in a new controlling shareholder to buy out public market investors and give him protection. He got a new board, and the plan was to take the company private. He thought the new board—because they understood retail and understood him, looking him in the eye and saying, "I see you, man"—would surely back him up. But they didn't. The new board didn't solve anything because they were still under the hypnotic power of these "best practices." They wanted higher prices, lower wages, and faster growth, and they didn't care about the collateral damage to employees or customers. They just wanted to see the numbers go up. One day in 1975, Sol came into work and found they had changed the locks on his door. He couldn't even get into his office. He didn't work there anymore. Wow. So, just like Jeff Lawson and so many others, he lost control. What happened next is a natural A/B test in business history. We don't get these very often. In Branch A, FedMart's investors got what they wanted. They got Sol out of the way and converted FedMart to traditional business practices. It was bankrupt within seven years. Oh, wow. They destroyed in seven years what he took twenty years to build. Sol was a classic entrepreneur, like so many people we know. He took exactly two weeks off after this happened to lick his wounds, and then he was back at work. He even leased the office upstairs from FedMart. Nice. He leased it for his new company. It was just like, "F you, I'm doing it again." He created a new company called Price Club. When I was a kid, Price Club was a major retailer; my family shopped there all the time. But most people today don't know Price Club because of what happened next. One of the FedMart employees, a guy who had worked his way up from stockboy to executive, quit FedMart in protest when Sol was fired. He created his own company because he understood the engine just like Sol did. Later, the two companies merged to form Price Costco—which we now just call Costco. Makes sense. That is the deep-cut backstory of how Costco came to be. Costco today still embodies Sol Price's "fiduciary to the customer" idea, but it is protected by what I call a "governance fortress." This protects it from outside attacks so its board understands its job very differently than most corporate boards. Instead of saying, "Our job is just to maximize returns for shareholders," the board understands that their job is to protect the mission. When we talk about being mission-controlled, this is what we're aiming for. The book is loaded with techniques; I promise it's not just a manifesto, there's a lot of detail to it. We're not going to learn everything from a podcast, but overall, if you zoom way out, the pattern is this: we need an ethos like Sol Price had—some kind of higher principle that we're committed to, where we understand we'll make money by maximizing human flourishing. Then, we need structural integrity: the ability to protect that precious thing we've created from outside pressure. That's the short formula: Ethos + Integrity = Incorruptible. How do you find the right board members for something like that? I mean, there are the documents, which founders have control over when starting out. But even with the best documents in the world, without the right people, you still can't keep it going. Yeah. The first step, and by far the hardest step, is being willing to say that you are not in line with these "best practices." They represent what is called a normative consensus—everyone agrees that everyone agrees that this is the right way. You've got to agree that you're going to be punk rock. You're not going to... Yeah, you just have to say, "This is not for me." I tell a story in the book about Costco versus Kroger. Costco came under attack in the early 2000s for having non-standard governance practices. In fact, Costco routinely gets the worst possible rating from governance-rating agencies. Meanwhile, Kroger decided to go all-in on "best practices." This set up a natural experiment: Costco has been incredibly successful despite having "bad" governance, while Kroger has not seen the same level of success. One analyst even called Kroger's performance "Costco in reverse." So, I always tell people, the next time someone says, "I want you to adopt this best practice," you don't have to say anything out loud. Just think to yourself, "Best practice equals Kroger practice." That means someone wants you to be more like Kroger and less like Costco. Why would you want that? No shade on Kroger, but you could either be one of the greatest performing stocks of all time, or you could be this other thing. We have to master these techniques, and the most important thing is to create selection bias. You want investors and board members who choose to be with you because they believe in the mission, not just because they want to make a quick buck. Even if they are investors who will profit from you, you want to make sure their interests are truly aligned. I was just talking to a founder today who ran into a common problem. You put an awesome person on your board who you think walks on water, but you forget that you've taken an investment from a venture firm, not that individual. When that person leaves, you're stuck with a new person. Suddenly, you've given veto and control rights to someone you don't even know. You don't know if they're aligned with you, and you've probably never read the fund's LPA, so you don't know what their incentives are. I think founders are generally much too naive and credulous about who will be a good long-term partner. They are easily bamboozled by people who say, "I'm founder-friendly, we don't need to worry about this stuff, we're aligned." But then they add, "By the way, I also need the ability to fire you at will." You're like, "I thought you believed in my vision." And they reply, "Well, as long as I like what's going on." That is not what belief actually means. YC Startup School is back. What about on the legal document side? Let's say you get into YC and you've got a SAFE, but you haven't converted to equity yet. Yeah. The normal path with pure founder shares is to see if you can get supervoting, or write it in from day one. I guess the Delaware C-corp structure is a pretty big deal. A lot of AI companies have chosen to be PBCs (Public Benefit Corporations) on day one. What is the best practice for people starting out? Yeah, I think a PBC is an absolute must-do and an utter no-brainer. Of all the things in the book, it is by far the easiest. If you want to pick just one thing to do, this is it. It's a two-page legal filing in Delaware; your lawyer can have it done tomorrow. Especially if you only have SAFEs and no equity investors yet, you can just turn into a PBC tomorrow. You don't even need anyone to agree; you just do it. What is interesting about the PBC—and most people get confused because they think it's the same as the "B Corp" certification you see at the farmer's market, which is totally different—is that it simply restores what is called purposeful incorporation. For the vast majority of the centuries we've had joint-stock corporations on this planet, everyone understood and thought it was completely obvious that companies should be incorporated to do a specific thing. So, literally a mission or a public benefit. It should have a public benefit. If you look at 19th-century companies and read their charters, none of them say "maximize shareholder value." That would have been considered a crime. Interesting, I did not know that. Their charters would say something like, "We have created this entity to build a railroad" or "to build a canal from place to place"—to do something specific. The board's responsibility, their fiduciary duty and highest priority, was to defend and protect that purpose. When we shifted to shareholder primacy, we forgot this incredibly important lesson. So, who did this? Who did this to us? Who did this to us? It's actually wild. Let's do a quick history lesson, if you're okay with that. I would love to, yeah. This is incredible to me. There is a story in the book about a 19th-century situation where, if you know Elon's takeover of Twitter, you'll think, "I know how this goes." One of the richest men in the world tried to buy and take over the Erie Canal Company, which built and operated the canal. He had unlimited money, so he decided, "No matter what it takes, I'm going to buy this company and convert it to do what I want." But the board of directors, unlike modern boards who are told they must become auctioneers and sell, said, "We will fight you by whatever means necessary." Both sides went crazy; it was an intense fight, and there were no corporate governance or ethical standards like we have now. There are stories of both sides bribing the same state legislators, and politicians taking bribes from both sides before voting with one of them. It was an epic fight. But if you read the commentary from back then, while people were scandalized by the illegal tactics, nobody said, "It's wrong that the board tried to fight this." Everyone agreed, "Of course they tried to fight; that makes perfect sense." In the end, he lost. They fought him off using a poison pill tactic—which today you can just ask your lawyer to put in your documents if you don't already have it. Why not? That's another tactic. You can just have so many of these things. You don't have to wait for permission; you just do it. If he had prevailed and managed to convert the legal purpose of the company from building a canal to maximizing shareholder value, that would have been considered a crime. The courts would have voided the company's charter, giving them the corporate death penalty, because it would have been considered beyond the scope of what was authorized for the public benefit. This was the rule across the US through the end of the 19th century. The problem was that to get one of these charters, you had to get your state legislature to grant it. Imagine having to lobby your local state senate just for permission to form a company. It was incredibly cumbersome, and that part of the system was bad. So, there was a major fight over the 19th century to establish general incorporation—the simple idea that anyone who wants to should be able to form a company for any reason. It had to be fought statehouse by statehouse, and eventually, every state came into compliance. The key date for our purposes is when Delaware adopted this in 1899. This is relatively recent history. Even in 1899, if you read the Delaware rule, it said you could have a company for any purpose, but it still assumed you would have a specific purpose or mission. That was considered completely obvious. Yet, over the course of the 20th century, companies were increasingly advised by lawyers to put a general purpose in their documents instead of writing in a specific one. Most founders have never read their corporate charter and have no idea what it says. Shame on you; go read it! You're going to read it and say, "Eric, what are you talking about? It says here that the Acme AI Company is incorporated to pursue..." and there is a blank space like a Mad Lib where someone wrote "any legal act or activity." You think that means anything goes, so it's all good. Wrong. It is so wrong, and we're going to get into why. In the '60s and '70s, a small group of academics, judges, and legal scholars decided that "any lawful act or activity" actually means shareholder primacy. Unlike the general incorporation rules they were replacing, they never put this to a vote. In the history of the world, shareholder primacy has never been subject to any referendum or legislative action. It's weird; if you learned in school how a bill becomes a law, you'll find there is no actual law for shareholder primacy. Yeah. Yet, if you ask any director on any board in America, "What is your first duty?" they will say, "To maximize returns for shareholders." So how can it be the law and yet not a law? Well, the courts just decided. Guys like Milton Friedman wrote famous op-eds claiming "the social purpose of a corporation is to increase its profits." Notice they didn't say, "We have regular corporations and then we have B-corps." They said, "The purpose of a corporation is to increase its profits." The trick was they convinced everyone this is how it has always been, and we all bought into it. Well, it turns out we don't even need to get the law changed. We just have to start saying, "No, we don't think this makes sense. We don't want to be part of this." If you read the legal papers—and I'll give you one last funny detail that blew my mind—these legal scholars have to write papers explaining why this is the law if it's not actually on the books. They write things that are hilarious, like, "Shareholder primacy is a legal obligation, not a legal duty." Oh. Subtle. Subtle to people. What? They say, "Look, at the end of the day, even though this is not technically a law, it is the law, and it's okay for the courts to enforce it like one." Remember, you will be fired if you violate it. It really is the law. And if you're sued about it, you will lose. Yeah, you will lose that lawsuit. The board of Twitter was forced to sue Elon to complete that transaction, even though they weren't happy about it, because they felt they had a fiduciary duty to do it for the shareholders. So different. If they hadn't done it, they would have been sued, and the lawsuit definitely would have succeeded. So, they weren't wrong about their requirements. But why would we build companies like this? Who wants to be taken over at the barrel of a gun? If you read these papers, they basically conclude that shareholder primacy is a normative consensus, meaning everyone agrees that everyone agrees that this is how companies should act. I work with a lot of founders, and I love to ask them, "Are you part of this normative consensus? Does this seem right to you?" Every founder I've ever met says, "Certainly not." So I ask, "Have you ever told even one other human being that you're not part of this consensus, until just now?" And they're like, "Oh, no, you can't say stuff like that out loud." But you can! You can say it out loud. In fact, if there's a controversy, it can't be a consensus, now can it? You can just say it. Luckily, we have the PBC and other tools in our arsenal where we can formally and legally declare this as founders. So we don't have to have a social movement per se, but we could, because it would be fun. We could! And in the book, I call this the "builder's intuition"—the belief that the best way to make money is to create more value than you capture, to build something people want. Tim O'Reilly, Paul Graham, and all the legends of our industry agree on this and talk about it all the time. Yet, we're all supposed to pretend these days that all ways of making money are equally good, even though there are so many ways to get rich today without creating any value at all. Yeah. Why don't we just stop pretending that we think that's good? None of us think it is. We think it's all BS. We should start reclaiming our identity as builders and say, "You know what? We are not part of this normative consensus. We don't want to do this anymore. We want a different solution." If we just say, "Because investor control isn't working, we should have founder control," that's not great either. I know founders who are basically trapped; they can never quit because they are the literal human shield blocking everything. That's too much pressure, and eventually, people die. Then what? If you want to build a truly long-term solution, we have to look for structural solutions that do not depend on the goodwill of a single person. We have to think about it like building a government with checks and balances, balancing faction against faction. Luckily, there are great precedents for this. Interesting. So, if you become a PBC and you have a specific mission, does that mean you are no longer subject to being removed if you make choices that don't maximize shareholder value? Correct. Does that also mean you can be removed if you are not working towards the mission? Unfortunately, no. It doesn't quite work both ways. The problem is that directors, especially under Delaware law, have extremely wide latitude to do whatever they want as long as they can justify it as being in line with the charter. In this case, if your directors are under pressure from investors to fire you because you're not maximizing shareholder value, the PBC can act as a shield they can use to protect themselves from being sued. They can say, "Look, we're working towards this mission." Unfortunately, if they decide to fire you anyway, the PBC doesn't help you there; you can't sue them and say, "What the F?" They still get to make their own business judgment. A huge part of the problem is we are taught that "best practice" is to have a combination of investor directors and independent directors. Today's definition of good governance is: the more independent directors, the better. Why is that? The theory is reasonable: since independent directors are independent, they have no stake in the outcome. For those who don't know, "independent" means they have no equity in the company and are not aligned with you at all. So they're basically random, eminent people. The problem is they have no financial incentive for the mission to endure, but they do have an incentive to be seen as pro-investor. How do you get independent director jobs? You get recommended by investors. Founders, you have to do a better job at this. Most founders never recommend anyone for a director role, whereas investors do a great job of it. If you have a classic Silicon Valley board of two VCs, two founders, and one independent, that is supposed to be a fair balance. But in reality, investors already control your company. Right. The research, by the way—and there's a little chart in chapter nine of the book called "Best Practices Destroy Shareholder Value"—shows that independent directors do not accomplish their intended goals because they face an actual conflict of interest despite their nominal independence. The solution is to realize that just as investor directors are doubly accountable—a GP of a venture fund on your board has a duty to both the company and their LPs, which doesn't bother anyone—we can apply checks to independent directors. They currently aren't accountable to anyone except themselves. But we can create a second entity, like a two-branch government, where outside trustees have the responsibility of appointing directors. This structure has been proven to be far more stable than the so-called best practice of a single entity run as a Delaware C-corp. Can I tell you a story? Yeah. This is one of my favorite stories. I'll tell you the premise, you won't believe me, and then I'll prove that it's true. The premise is a story about a time when the nonprofit directors of a company created more than $500 billion of shareholder value. In the 1920s, a woman named Marie Krogh was living in Denmark when she received a fatal diagnosis of diabetes, which had no known cure at the time. Today, Marie is mostly famous because of her husband, August Krogh, who had just won the Nobel Prize. He asked her if, despite her illness, she would accompany him on a lecture tour of North America. She agreed. While they were in North America meeting scientists and giving lectures, a scientist told them at dinner one night that someone in Canada had figured out how to isolate insulin for the first time. Oh. It was a potential cure. They were excited, and it was obvious they had to go see it. They could have easily just asked, "Can you send us some doses?" but they wanted to see it for themselves. They met the Canadians, saw the possibility, and asked to license the technology to commercialize it in Denmark. But both they and the Canadians had a major worry—decades ahead of Martin Shkreli. Yeah. They thought, "Wait a minute. If we're a for-profit company selling a life-saving medicine... if you sell a medicine to me that I need to live, I want you to charge me a fair price, stay in business, and have an incentive to keep producing it. But I would live in fear that one day you'd wake up like Martin Shkreli and realize, 'If Eric owes his life to this medicine, can't I charge him whatever I want? He is basically my slave.'" They foresaw this in the 1920s. When they went home to Denmark, they agreed to build the Nordisk Insulin Laboratorium as a for-profit subsidiary of a non-profit foundation. Wow. So they built two entities instead of one. The non-profit had trustees, and the for-profit had directors. It's a great MVP story: they produced their first batch of insulin within three months of returning to Denmark. They got straight to work because it was an urgent problem to save Marie's life and the lives of others. If the Nordisk Insulin Laboratorium sounds familiar, it should—it is the predecessor to Novo Nordisk. This company has been going for over a hundred years with its scientific integrity fully intact. People hear this and assume they were just lucky or that Danes are uniquely friendly, but that's not true. The same forces that came for Jeff Lawson, Sol Price, and countless tech companies absolutely came for Novo Nordisk as they grew. In the early 2000s, there was a major wave of "best practices" advising pharmaceutical companies to merge and grow larger. The for-profit board and independent directors at Novo Nordisk agreed they had to merge. They found a partner, signed an agreement, and were set to get a massive premium on the stock price. Since Novo was publicly traded, the final checklist item was getting the foundation's permission. The trustees asked, "What is the purpose of this transaction? Our job is to protect the mission, and we can only approve a merger if it's necessary for the company's survival." They actually had to hold two meetings. The board huddled with their bankers and came back saying, "Since survival is the standard, this is the new best practice in pharma—it's eat or be eaten. If we don't merge, we'll die." The non-profit trustees responded, "That's interesting, but what problem are we solving exactly? Novo Nordisk has been profitable for ten years in a row and is growing at 20% a year. It doesn't need to do anything." Why do we have to do it? Why can't we just be a great business doing our thing? Anyway, long story short, the trustees said no, and the merger was over. Yeah. People were incredibly pissed because it was a $20 billion deal that would have made a lot of money for bankers and investors. But this set up a rare counterfactual in business history. We know for sure that if the merger had gone through, Novo Nordisk's major R&D programs would have been canceled. We know because the company they were going to merge with was bought by Merck two years later, and that's exactly what happened. Oh my god. One of those research programs was in year eleven of a thirteen-year journey to invent GLP-1. Because the trustees intervened, the program survived. GLP-1 was so difficult to develop that even ten years in, they had no proof it would work, and people questioned why they were still funding it. But they kept the faith. Fast-forward twenty years, and that single intervention allowed Novo Nordisk to reach a market valuation greater than the entire GDP of Denmark. And if you freeze-frame right at that moment, you will have—now notice the delta between what they would have sold for and what they actually are worth is more... Short-term versus long-term, which is a theme over and over. Over and over again. They became—they, they, I think, I think their valuation crested at $600 billion. I think, though, that builders have to take some responsibility here, okay? I mentioned before that most founders have never read their corporate governing documents. Worth doing. Everyone's just like, "Well, my lawyer will take care of it." It's like governance—I hate even using the word governance because as soon as people hear that, people are just like, "Boring." Who cares? I mean, I remember with Lean Startup. Lean Startup is a book about management. I remember telling people like, "It's going to talk about management," and they'd be like, "Management? So boring." Like, yeah. So because we delegate this to lawyers and bankers and whoever else, when it comes back to bite us in the butt, we're like totally unprepared. And I think builders have to get a lot more savvy about this. First of all, make sure they understand what they're signing, but then also, yeah, to put on the t-shirt with the fist to be like, "I don't want this to happen to me. Like, I, this is not the kind of company I want to build." And I think we are not that far from being able to have an economy that is about building again. Like, I really think we are close to that. The younger generations are super pissed off. This idea of shareholder primacy has had its 40, 50-year run now. People who have grown up only under that system have seen its failure. Like, we live in a time of institutional collapse and institutional weakness. And this is the thing: like, when companies collapse, when they go through these ethical moral collapses, it's also an economic collapse. Like, that's what's so interesting to me is it's value-destroying. So we don't need to make a moral argument necessarily; we can just make an economic argument, and the book is loaded with the evidence. For example, the Novo Nordisk style of company where you have this two-tiered foundation—that's called an industrial foundation structure in the academic literature. Companies with that structure, there's a lot of them. The German optics company Zeiss had this structure in 1885. Oh, wow. So it's not even that new. There's enough of these companies that we have a data set to see how they perform. Companies with this structure are six times more likely to live to year 50. Amazing. 10% versus 60% probability. So we as founders, when we're choosing our corporate form, we're being told that there's this business monoculture. All you can do is a Delaware C corp—everything standard, best practice, best practice. Man, we've been deprived of our birthright. There's way more options out there than we're being told. And I just, I always tell founders, "Why are you hearing about it from me? Why didn't your investors tell you? Why didn't your lawyers tell you? Like, how come you, if you don't know this story, why not? Why wasn't it in your MBA class? Why is this not in the curriculum?" Well, we need VC funds to not be 10-year funds. Well, that's a huge problem. Just for the people, the audience, I mean, basically, the standard LP agreement for a venture capital fund is 10 years, which also means that at the end of those 10 years, the money that was put out is expected to come back. So, 10 years is... It's just not that long anymore. Especially, I mean, it made sense; most of these practices come from a time when companies would go public like three years after being founded. Amazon went public I think that way, raised like $20 million. Like, it just—the scale of an IPO was much less of a big deal back then. It used to be the three-year overnight success, now it's the 20-year overnight success. Absolutely. And, of course, we have companies like Stripe that are like stubbornly just refusing to go public, because why should they? And I think people wring their hands about this, but it's like, "What do you expect, man?" If you create a system that puts this gravitational pressure on companies, you have to expect people to fight back and resist. I don't think the tools we've used in our resistance are very good. I think we've like been desperately grabbing for whatever we can grab because we feel like... More and more founder control, which is, I mean, honestly, what we advocate for. It's better than nothing. I mean, it's certainly better than investor control, which really is self-defeating. Founder control has all these problems. And what's interesting to me is people who do founder control have no bridge; they're stuck. So like, for example, one of the things I advocate for is that if you're going to do founder control, just write into the docs that if the founder control is ever defeated for any reason, there's an alternative thing that springs up in its place. You can, you can write this in your docs right now. You can have this at the seed stage; it's not that hard. Just say, "Yes, I'm going to have the Novo Nordisk industrial foundation structure, or I'm going to have one of..." There's a bunch of structures that I, I explain in the book. What's cool about that is founders think that having dual-class shares makes you invincible, but it really doesn't. Dual-class is defeated all the time. I give a bunch of examples in the book, like, you know, stock price drops, everyone panics, founder gets forced out anyway because at the end of the day, like, having the votes is not the only thing that matters. There's a bunch of things that matter, including investors being like, "Well, we won't give you any more money unless you turn this protection off." So, I think the perception of being invincible and like being emperor for life, not only is it not true—those people still get betrayed—but you also, it has a psychological effect that's not healthy. It's called hubris syndrome in the, in the psychological literature. It makes you less generous, less compassionate, it makes you more selfish, it makes you more afraid, conversely, of losing your power. So if you notice some of these like emperor-for-life billionaire types out on social media like having a mental breakdown in front of us all... Like, part of what's going on is that like this is a mental illness. This is not actually a good structure. Because we have these better structures, there's really no reason to do it, or at least do it as a backup for after your dual-class shares get defeated, which, you know, in general speaking, they eventually do get defeated, including by the death of the founder. So what then? Like, I think if you want to build a 50-year or 100-year company, you got to last longer than... One of the more profound reasons to start a company is actually wanting to create something that outlives you. I think so. I think that's actually part of why entrepreneurship is so awesome, like, as a career. Well, the first time I ever heard of a PBC actually was from my friend Scott Phoenix. He started an AI lab called Vicarious, and he very explicitly said, "I'm going to do it as a PBC. We're going to try to create AGI. And if we create AGI, we don't want to be, you know, sort of forced into a paperclip maximization world where you have AGI and it's self-improving, and then anyone, any shareholder, could come and say, 'Actually, we're not maximizing enough.'" And then you helped design the long-term benefit trust for Anthropic. Yeah. So, I'm curious. I mean, what was that like? Oh, it was wild. Yeah, and listen, I do not take credit for Anthropic's success. Okay? Just for the record, like, I played only a bit part, and all credit to Dario and Daniela and the whole team. They're on such a run. Love them. But if you talk to people about why is Anthropic so successful, they'll often say something like, "Well, their inference costs are lower." Oh, that's definitely not true right now. Or, whatever, you know, whatever it is, right? They have, they have better, superior focus. The models are fantastic. Models are better. What, but like if you ask why, it'll be like, "Well, they have better technical architecture." But why? "Well, because they're better at doing this thing and that thing." But why? Ultimately, it's about its people. Yeah, it's like because they have the best talent. Why is that? "Well, because the best people want to work there." But why? "Because they think they're the good guys." That's like a huge recruiting talent advantage; it is unbelievable. So many incredible people want to work there because they are—like, you ask about the focus, why are they so aligned? Because everyone's on mission, because the mission is primary. But why has Anthropic been able to be bullied from the outside? People certainly have tried. I think part of the reason is they have that magic formula: Ethos + Integrity. They have this ethos of AI safety, whether you agree with it or not, they really believe it. I remember, I met them—now they're world beaters, I know—but I remember meeting them when they had just left OpenAI. The team was very aligned, the people who were, who were leaving, who were leaving a really lucrative job behind to do this. So, people really were there for the mission. They had what they thought were really aligned investors, including Sam Bankman-Fried, so actually turned out not so much to be, but they thought—that's what they thought at the time. It would have been that whole situation whole, apparently. The stake that the bankruptcy has of those shares is worth more than the whole, than all, the entire fraud by a lot, and it's going to be worth even more when, when the time comes. Yeah, it's like one of the most bizarre situations in history. It's pretty wild. And in spite of having so carefully curated their cap table—like, one of the most tightly curated cap tables I've ever seen for a company like this—a huge chunk of the company wound up being sold at auction in a bankruptcy auction. Like, how crazy is that? So anyway, people always think, "If I just curate, if I just choose the right people..." Well, Anthropic had. But they were also really worried about this paperclip maximizer thing, like, what happens if we're sold to the highest bidder? What happens? Well, they understood this technology is going to be worth trillions if it works. The incentive to take it over is going to be unbelievable, so we need a structure strong enough. And so we talked, just like we're talking now; we had the same conversation then that we're having now. Again, credit to them for actually doing the work, but I remember working with them on their charter, and they had to defend this idea for two years because they kept writing it into the term sheets that they would do it, would do it, would do it, but it took time to, like, figure out what they wanted to do. I think it was the Series C when they finally established this thing called the Long-Term Benefit Trust, which is not a non-profit foundation, it's actually what's called a perpetual purpose trust, which is a different legal category but the same idea: outside trustees who have the power to appoint directors to the for-profit board. People very frequently ask me, "Why is Anthropic the most courageous of the AI labs?" I think this is part of the reason why. They have this structural strength to stand up for what they believe, and if you notice, when companies stand up and do the right thing, we live in such a polarized time. It's like, "What does it mean, the right thing? Are you saying they're absolutely right, they're morally perfect?" No. If you want me to criticize Anthropic, I can think of lots of things. When I say they did the right thing, they acted consistent with their own values. Those values are consistent with human flourishing, and they have the strength to defend them. So I know a lot of people, even in San Francisco—San Francisco's in a lot of turmoil right now, tech companies are not the most popular here right now, AI companies maybe even less so. When Anthropic turned down that $200 million contract, someone sent me a video: the sidewalks around their headquarters had been chalked up with people saying, "Thank you." Oh, wow. Yeah. And when I tell you that a San Francisco tech company had its sidewalk chalked up, "Thank you" is not the kind of language you're expecting. So like, because they have this strength, they get all these counterintuitive benefits. Claude went to number one when they did that. They couldn't have known that was going to happen, they couldn't, like, and who knows what's going to happen next, obviously a long way to go with Anthropic and with the question of AGI. But I think the early returns are very promising, that just taking a little bit of extra time to set the structure up in a thoughtful way has been incredibly valuable to their success. Eric, thank you so much for joining us. This is truly epic. Oh, it's an honor. I, I really think this is a message that YC founders need to hear and pay attention to, so I'm just, I'm really excited to see this finally get out in the world. Good to be with you.

Walmart & Target Signal Consumer Stress as the 10-Year Yield Hits 4.6% | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(174.0K)·20:22·40.8K views·2026-05-22
Steve EismanHedge Fund Manager and Host, The Weekly Wrap
TL;DRSteve Eisman has reduced his market exposure ("lightened up") after the 10-year Treasury yield breached his 4.5% "Rubicon" to hit 4.6%, driven by rising inflation, geopolitical tensions, and emerging signs of consumer weakness from retail giants Walmart and Target.

TL;DR

Steve Eisman has reduced his market exposure ("lightened up") after the 10-year Treasury yield breached his 4.5% "Rubicon" to hit 4.6%, driven by rising inflation, geopolitical tensions, and emerging signs of consumer weakness from retail giants Walmart and Target.

***

Macroeconomic Outlook and the Interest Rate "Rubicon"

  • Strong S&P 500 Performance: Eisman rules out an imminent recession, citing robust S&P 500 Q1 fundamentals that handily beat expectations.
    • Revenue Growth: Actual revenue growth reached 11.1%, significantly outperforming the 7.3% to 9.0% expectations set earlier in the year.
    • Earnings Growth: S&P 500 earnings growth skyrocketed to 28.3%, nearly double the forecasted 14.4%.
  • The 4.5% Interest Rate Boundary: Despite strong corporate growth, Eisman has turned cautious because the 10-year Treasury yield breached 4.5% (reaching 4.6%).
    • Inflation Concerns: Elevated CPI and PPI data, compounded by rising oil prices from Middle Eastern geopolitical tensions, are keeping upward pressure on yields. Eisman anticipates that upcoming inflation reports will not be benign.

Retail and Housing: Early Warning Signs of Consumer Stress

  • Retail Giants Signal Weakness: Both Target and Walmart reported earnings dynamics that indicate a squeezing consumer.
    • Target: Despite beating EPS ($1.71 vs. $1.30 expected) and posting a strong 5.6% same-store sales increase, the stock fell 7%. Management warned of future headwinds from fading tax refund benefits and high oil prices.
    • Walmart: Revenue beat, but EPS only met expectations at $0.66. Crucially, Walmart issued weak Q2 EPS guidance ($0.72–$0.74 vs. the $0.75 expected), citing the same consumer pressures as Target.
  • Sluggish Housing and Building Sector: Rising interest rates continue to severely depress residential real estate and building products.
    • Home Depot: EPS fell 4% year-over-year to $3.43. Same-store sales grew an anemic 0.6%, missing the 0.8% expectation.
    • Lowe’s: Reported similar lackluster fundamentals, with same-store sales matching Home Depot at a flat 0.6% growth rate.
    • Toll Brothers: The luxury homebuilder beat metrics and raised guidance, yet EPS was still down 22% year-over-year ($2.72 vs. $3.50 last year), indicating sector fundamentals remain at the bottom of the channel.

AI Disruption: Tech Winners and Software Losers

  • NVIDIA’s Unprecedented Scale: NVIDIA reported blockbuster Q1 results, yet the stock remained relatively flat after-hours due to extremely high "whisper numbers" despite beating consensus.
    • Revenue Acceleration: Total revenue of $81.6 billion was up 85% year-over-year.
    • Expanding Margins: Gross margins reached 75%, up from 60% in the same quarter last year. EPS surged 95% to $1.87.
  • Software Demolition: Incumbent software companies are facing severe downward re-ratings on fears of artificial intelligence disruption.
    • Salesforce: Downgraded to Underperform by Bank of America due to AI monetization anxieties, limited upsell potential, and slowing customer acquisition. The stock is down 35% year-to-date.
    • Intuit: Revenue growth slowed to 10% (its slowest expansion rate since 2024), and net income rose just 9%. The company announced it is laying off 17% of its workforce, driving the stock down double digits after-hours (down 50% year-to-date).

Utility M&A Driven by Data Center Demand

  • NextEra and Dominion Energy Merger: NextEra is acquiring Dominion Energy in an all-stock transaction to create the world's largest regulated electrical utility. NextEra will control 75% of the combined entity.
    • Data Center Tailwinds: Dominion powers Northern Virginia, the largest data center market globally, while NextEra is the nation's leading renewable energy developer.
    • Interest Rate Drag: Although data centers present a structural growth narrative for utilities, utility stocks act as bond proxies due to their high dividends. Rising interest rates have severely capped the sector's performance.

Private Credit Refinancing and "The Big Short" Execution

  • The Impending Private Credit Cliff: Eisman agrees with analyst assessments that AI disruption has not yet translated into credit stress, but warns a day of reckoning is coming for private equity-backed software firms.
    • Refinancing Pressures: Many lower-tier software companies purchased by private equity firms are down over 50% in value (similar to public leader ServiceNow's decline).
    • The 2027-2031 Pivot: When massive debts mature starting in 2027, private credit lenders will demand private equity firms inject substantial new equity. If they decline, private equity will have to "hand over the keys" of these devalued software companies to lenders.
  • Behind "The Big Short" Settlement: Addressing a viewer question on how his hedge fund closed out its historic 2008 subprime short position, Eisman clarified that the exit was not as theatrical as depicted in the film *The Big Short*.
    • The Execution: In spring 2008, fearing federal bailouts would reverse the trade, Eisman's team simply called trading desks at Goldman Sachs and Deutsche Bank, requested credit default swap (CDS) buyout prices, accepted the terms, and covered the positions normally.

***

"Just take a step back and consider the fact that the largest company in the United States by market cap grew its revenue by 85%. Amazing." — Steve Eisman on NVIDIA
"When [refinancing] happens, private credit will demand that private equity pony up more equity into their software companies that are worth now less than half. Private equity may choose to do so or hand over the keys." — Steve Eisman

Between work, banking, and shopping, I have too many logins to keep track of. Way too many logins. Not a day goes by that I have to log into something and I have completely forgotten my username and password, but I know that when I check with Keeper, my information will be right there. Keeper is a password manager that creates strong, unique passwords for all your accounts, stores them securely in one place, and logs you in automatically across all your devices so you never have to remember, guess, or worry about your login credentials again. For businesses, Keeper takes the chaos out of managing access for teams so they can securely share logins, control permissions, and protect sensitive data without slowing down day-to-day work. What people really notice is how simple it feels. Security features like autofill and Face ID or fingerprint login quietly make things easier so best-in-class protection doesn't feel like extra work, it just happens in the background. From individuals to large organizations, millions of people worldwide rely on Keeper to keep passwords organized and accounts protected, and I'm one of those people. That's keepersecurity.com/eisman. Moving on. Let's look at an interest rate-sensitive group. However, the entire group has underperformed the broader market by 1,200 basis points since earnings season kicked off about a month ago with concerns around higher rates and margins. Higher interest rates have hurt the homebuilders, Home Depot, Lowes, and the entire building supply group. Two mailbags this week. Our first mailbag is from YM, who says the following: "I'm part of a small team at Fitch Ratings that produces high-frequency credit commentary and cross-sector research. Your recent masterclass on private credit prompted me to reach out with a few observations." By the way, that masterclass is part of our premium product. "On the private credit discussion, while no consensus definition exists for the asset class, we generally consider it debt instruments without a readily tradeable secondary market. By that definition, Tricolor and First Brands were not actually private credit, as they were part of the broadly syndicated loan and ABS markets, though I'd agree they're prime examples of deteriorating underwriting standards. On BDCs specifically, one of your guests said, 'If you're worried about private credit, you should really be worried about private equity,' and I couldn't agree more. The recent BDC redemptions look more like a sentiment, liquidity, and valuation multiple issue than evidence of deteriorating credit quality. There's no clear signal yet of rising defaults in software driven by AI disruption – not because it won't happen, but because it is too early for AI disruption to convert to credit stress. I believe it will take a few years to play out, and the upcoming refinancings for debt maturing in 2028-2031 will be the real test." He goes on to say: "A question on a separate topic. You mentioned that US Treasuries are seen as the 'risk-free rate,' and corporate bond spreads have remained historically tight in recent years. This is typically interpreted as reflecting market optimism, but I wonder whether that is starting to also reflect a narrowing creditworthiness gap between the US sovereign and investment-grade corporates, as corporate balance sheets remain in great shape while federal debt continues to climb. At what point, in your view, do markets begin to reprice Treasuries away from the risk-free benchmark? And if they do, does the spread framework break down? That's a question that hasn't been broadly covered, and I am genuinely curious to hear your thoughts." My answer: With respect to software and private equity and private credit, I largely agree. It's still so early in the AI story that the businesses of the software incumbents have not yet experienced a lot of AI inroads. Despite the recent rally in software stocks, and it's only been a rally that's been going on for about a week or so, these stocks are still down enormously. For example, ServiceNow is still down 50% over the last year. The software companies purchased by private equity are almost certainly not as good as ServiceNow, so they are down at least 50% in value as well. The debt will need to be refinanced starting in 2027. When that happens, private credit will demand that private equity pony up more equity into their software companies that are worth now less than half. Private equity may choose to do so or hand over the keys. As far as credit spreads are concerned, I do not believe that spreads are tight because of a narrowing of creditworthiness between US sovereign debt and investment-grade corporations. Despite all the noise about fiat currency and the level of government debt, the US 10-year has been in a range of 3.9% to 4.5% for several years. The recent rise in Treasury yields has nothing to do with the creditworthiness of the US, in my view, and everything to do with bad inflation data. It's not only the knowledge and experience, but perhaps most of all your ability to clearly articulate your points in an easily understood manner. Great job and thanks!" You're welcome. Sundar Pichai Opening Remarks | I/O 2026 Keynote

Google(14.3M)·42:09·7.1K views·2026-05-22
Sundar PichaiCEO, Google
TL;DRGoogle has entered the "agentic era" of AI, driven by a massive infrastructure scaling up to $190 billion in capital expenditure and model usage processing 3.2 quadrillion tokens monthly.
Key announcements include the launch of the hyper-fast Gemini 3.5 Flash model, the...

TL;DR

Google has entered the "agentic era" of AI, driven by a massive infrastructure scaling up to $190 billion in capital expenditure and model usage processing 3.2 quadrillion tokens monthly. Key announcements include the launch of the hyper-fast Gemini 3.5 Flash model, the agent-first Antigravity 2.0 development platform, and Gemini Spark, a 24/7 personal consumer agent running continuously in the cloud.

Massive Scale and Next-Generation Infrastructure

  • Unprecedented Token Volume: Google's AI services now process 3.2 quadrillion tokens per month, a seven-fold increase from 480 trillion tokens at last year's I/O.
    • Developer model APIs process approximately 19 billion tokens per minute.
    • Over 375 enterprise customers each processed more than 1 trillion tokens over the past 12 months.
  • Massive User Growth: Google now has 13 products with over 1 billion monthly users, five of which exceed 3 billion users.
    • AI Overviews: Reached over 2.5 billion monthly users.
    • Gemini App: Exceeded 900 million monthly active users, more than doubling over the past year.
  • Infrastructure Capital Expenditure: Google expects its annual capex to reach $180 billion to $190 billion this year, up nearly six times from $31 billion in 2022.
  • 8th-Generation Custom TPUs: Google announced a dual-chip custom silicon strategy to optimize both training and inference:
    • TPU 8t (Training): Optimized for large-scale pre-training with three times the raw computing power of the previous generation. It leverages JAX and Pathways to seamlessly distribute training across multiple sites, scaling to a global cluster of over 1 million TPUs.
    • TPU 8i (Inference): Architected to minimize latency, delivering up to 1,500 tokens per second on upcoming Flash models and providing a two-fold improvement in performance-per-watt energy efficiency.

Next-Gen Frontier Models and Digital Provenance

  • Gemini Omni and World Simulation: Demis Hassabis (CEO, Google DeepMind) introduced Gemini Omni, a model family designed to understand and simulate physical reality (e.g., kinetic energy and gravity).
    • Video Generation & Editing: Omni generates highly accurate video from text prompts (e.g., claymation explainers of protein folding) and allows users to edit video elements in real time using natural language.
    • Gemini Omni Flash: The first model of the Omni family is available starting today, with Omni Pro coming soon.
  • Gemini 3.5 Flash: Google's newest mainstream model combines frontier-level capabilities with extreme action speed.
    • Performance: Outperforms Gemini 3.1 Pro across major benchmarks, including a massive leap in "GDP-val" (real-world economic tasks).
    • Speed and Cost: Operates four times faster than comparable frontier models at less than half the price. Google estimates that migrating 80% of enterprise workloads from other frontier models to 3.5 Flash could save top Cloud customers over $1 billion annually.
  • Expanded AI Transparency & Safety: Sundar Pichai announced advancements in watermarking and deepfake detection:
    • SynthID Expansion: Google’s invisible digital watermark has now protected over 100 billion images/videos and 60,000 years of audio. Industry leaders OpenAI, Kakao, and ElevenLabs are officially adopting SynthID.
    • C2PA Content Credentials: Standardized credentials showing image origin (e.g., edited with AI vs. captured on a physical camera) are rolling out across Google Search and Chrome.

Antigravity 2.0: Agent-First Software Engineering

  • Standalone Agentic IDE: Varun (Antigravity Lead) introduced Antigravity 2.0, a desktop application built from the ground up for agentic development, focusing on multi-agent orchestration, agent-produced artifacts, and sub-agent teamwork.
    • Speed Optimization: Within the Antigravity ecosystem, Gemini 3.5 Flash has been optimized to run 12 times faster.
  • Building an Operating System from Scratch: To demonstrate the platform’s capacity, Google developers tasked Antigravity agents with building a fully functional operating system.
    • Execution: Working asynchronously over 12 hours, 93 parallel sub-agents made 15,000 model requests and processed 2.6 billion tokens.
    • Outcome: The agents successfully built a working OS (complete with scheduler, memory management, and file systems) for less than $1,000 in API credits. Engineers successfully ran a text-based locomotive animation and the game *Doom* on the agent-built OS.
  • Availability: Antigravity 2.0 is globally available today, featuring a full CLI, an SDK, native voice support, and integrations with Firebase, Android, and Google AI Studio.

Gemini Spark: Continuous Personal AI Agents

  • 24/7 Background Execution: Josh (Gemini Product Lead) introduced Gemini Spark, a highly secure personal AI agent designed to execute complex, multi-step digital workflows on behalf of the user.
    • Spark runs on dedicated virtual machines in Google Cloud, meaning it operates continuously in the background even when a user closes their laptop or locks their phone.
  • Productivity Capabilities:
    • Gmail & Docs: Instantly drafts emails using custom skills (e.g., a `/ghostwriter` command to mimic the user’s personal tone) by pulling context from Google Drive and calendar data.
    • Collaborative Planning: Can parse invitations, update RSVP sheets dynamically from emails, and generate customized slide decks.
    • On-the-Go Integration: Supports voice-activated multitasking on Android and iOS devices, automatically parsing one long vocal brainstorm into separate, organized background tasks.
  • Pricing and Rollout:
    • Releasing to trusted testers immediately, with a public US beta launching next week for Google AI Ultra subscribers.
    • Google is introducing a new Ultra plan for $100/month, while dropping the price of its top-tier Ultra plan from $250/month to $200/month.
    • Gemini Spark will integrate directly into Chrome as an "agentic browser" this summer, followed by a dedicated Android hub, Android Halo, later this year.

Notable Quotes

"It's been 10 years since we pivoted the company to be AI-first. We knew how profound AI would be to advancing our mission and improving people's lives at scale. This is why we're taking a differentiated, full-stack approach to AI innovation." — Sundar Pichai
"Over the past year, AI capabilities have leaped forwards. We now have agents that can plan and act on our behalf, and Artificial General Intelligence is just a few years away." — Demis Hassabis
"We've moved beyond AI tools that help us write, to agents that help us act. These agents have lowered the barrier to development so much that anyone can be a builder, even busy CEOs." — Varun

Supporting all of this at scale for our users, while also serving enterprises and developers around the world, requires massive investments in infrastructure. And we've been investing for today and for the future. In 2022, we were spending $31 billion annually in capex. This year, we expect that number to be about six times that, approximately $180 to $190 billion. A key part of this investment is our custom silicon. A decade ago, we announced our very first commercial Tensor Processing Unit, or TPU, on this I/O stage. Since then, we have transformed how the industry builds for AI. We recently announced our eighth generation of TPUs at Cloud Next. For the first time, we have taken a dual-chip approach with specialized architectures for training and inference: TPU 8t and 8i. While they may look similar, they are actually pretty different. The 8t is optimized for large-scale pre-training, and it has nearly three times the raw computing power of our previous generation. We have taken a fundamentally different approach with our training infrastructure. With JAX and Pathways, our training is no longer constrained by the limits of a single massive data center. Instead, we can now seamlessly distribute training across multiple sites, scaling across more than 1 million TPUs globally. This gives us the ability to create the largest training cluster in the world. For model builders, this means training larger, more capable models in weeks rather than months. TPU 8i is designed for inference. We have dramatically improved speed at every step, because if we learned anything in 27 years of working on Search, it's that latency matters. It almost took longer to write out the request, and the game is pretty fun, too. In addition to speed, we are also thinking about scaling sustainably. Both chips are more energy efficient, delivering up to two times better performance-per-watt. TPUs have been hard at work training for I/O this year. I'm told we have a behind-the-scenes look. As generative AI gets better, so does the need for greater transparency. Research shows people can correctly identify high-quality deepfake videos only about a quarter of the time. Three years ago, we launched SynthID, our watermark that is invisible to the naked eye. Since launch, SynthID has now watermarked over 100 billion images and videos, along with 60,000 years of audio assets. Millions of people are using our SynthID detector in the Gemini app to verify AI-generated content. We are now going a step further and adding C2PA content credentials verification across products. This will show you if the origin of the content was AI or a camera, and if it's been edited with generative AI tools. In this example, Gemini can tell this photo was captured with a Pixel camera, and then edited with Google Photos. We want more people to have easy access to these tools, so we are expanding both SynthID and content credentials verification to Search and Chrome. You can simply circle to search or right-click in Chrome and ask, "Was this generated with AI?" and you'll get a clear response along with other helpful context. For example, this image was making the rounds on social media last year. It's obviously fake; I don't eat hamburgers. It might not be as clear to everyone else, and that's where these tools can be really useful. Of course, this only works at scale if more partners decide to watermark their own AI-generated content. Over 12 hours, 93 sub-agents working in parallel made over 15,000 model requests and processed 2.6 billion tokens to take an initially empty project to the core of a functioning operating system. This was not possible on Gemini 3.1 Pro. But thanks to the performance and cost efficiency of Gemini 3.5 Flash, developing an entirely functional operating system consumed less than $1,000 of API credits. Sundar Pichai on Whether Google Is Falling Behind in A.I.

Hard Fork(58.7K)·28:44·25.8K views·2026-05-22
Sundar PichaiCEO, Google
TL;DRGoogle CEO Sundar Pichai acknowledges that while Google remains a frontier leader in multimodal AI, it currently lags behind competitors in agentic coding, but he remains highly confident that Gemini 1.5 Pro scaling, systematic search evolutions,…
and robust hardware...

TL;DR

Google CEO Sundar Pichai acknowledges that while Google remains a frontier leader in multimodal AI, it currently lags behind competitors in agentic coding, but he remains highly confident that Gemini 1.5 Pro scaling, systematic search evolutions, and robust hardware infrastructure will secure Google's long-term leadership.

## The AI Frontier and Gemini 1.5 Pro

  • Competitive Standing: Google is at the frontier of text, voice, reasoning, and multimodality, but Pichai admits the company is currently behind competitors like Anthropic (with Claude Code and Cursor) in agentic coding, instruction following, and executing long-horizon tasks.
  • Gemini 1.5 Pro Rollout: Released to address developer pain points, the model has seen rapid internal adoption, with Google's developer usage doubling every week.
      • Pichai notes early user complaints regarding regressions, pricing, and strict usage limits, explaining that limits were intentionally tightened to prevent outages but will be quickly eased alongside post-training fixes.
  • Organizational Focus: Pichai rejects the idea that Google is spreading its resources too thin compared to hyper-focused startups.
      • He argues that Google's massive scale allows it to focus on multiple breakthroughs simultaneously, noting that "30 to 60 days look like five years" in the current industry cycle.

## Evolution of Search and the Business Model

  • Methodical Search Transition: Google is intentionally pacing its rollout of AI Overviews rather than abruptly replacing traditional "ten blue links."
      • User Experience: Pichai stresses the importance of bringing users along on the journey step-by-step, ensuring search remains fast while consistently providing source links to connect users directly to the web.
  • Monetization Strategy: Pichai expresses confidence in Google's financial future, anticipating a sustainable balance between subscriptions and advertising.
      • Economic Value: He argues that as AI agents perform increasingly complex, high-value tasks for consumers, the overall economic utility—and therefore the revenue potential—will naturally expand.

## Public Anxiety, Job Disruption, and the Singularity

  • Addressing Public Backlash: Responding to a poll showing only 16% of people view AI as "mostly good" (with 35% viewing it as "mostly bad"), Pichai labels this anxiety as completely natural.
      • Economic Anxiety: Because citizens are hearing that jobs will radically change or disappear, Pichai believes democratic engagement and public concern are healthy forces that will drive necessary societal action.
  • The Future of Work: Pichai argues that AI will augment productivity rather than trigger mass unemployment, using historical and medical analogies:
      • The Spreadsheet Analogy: Much like spreadsheets changed the baseline starting point for financial analysis, AI coding tools will democratize software development.
      • Healthcare Burnout: AI tools will handle administrative overhead for doctors, freeing them to spend more direct, quality time with patients.
      • The Radiologist Paradigm: As digital scans generate 10x more data than past film-based methods, AI is a necessity just to keep up with clinical demands.
  • The Timeline to AGI: Addressing Google DeepMind CEO Demis Hassabis's comment that humanity is in the "foothills of the singularity," Pichai aligns with Hassabis's 2030 timeline.
      • He notes that the rapid pace of progress over the past two years suggests AGI is "on the closer side than not," likely arriving in a 3-to-10-year window.

## Regulation, Safety, and the Compute Economy

  • Federal AI Regulation: Heading to the White House for an Executive Order signing, Pichai supports government efforts to balance innovation with oversight.
      • He advocates for cross-industry and government coordination, particularly in sharing cybersecurity vulnerabilities and establishing joint consortia.
  • Recursive Self-Improvement (RSI): While Google’s AlphaProof can solve complex International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) problems from scratch in 12 hours, Pichai states true RSI is not yet here.
      • He warns that approaching real RSI will require broad cross-industry consultations to avoid dangerous "race conditions" toward AGI.
  • TPU and Compute Sales: Despite the fierce race for compute, Google continues to sell TPU access to rivals like Anthropic.
      • Business Unit Independence: Pichai explains that Google Cloud operates as an independent business unit with its own cash flows and capacity planning.
      • Strategic Advantages: Providing TPUs to external researchers scales Google's hardware ecosystem, lowers manufacturing costs, and provides critical feedback loops to optimize Google's next-generation silicon.

Notable Quotes

"I think we are in the moment in time in this field where 30 to 60 days look like five years, that's all it is."

— Sundar Pichai

"I think people's concerns are a bit more fundamental around the shift... I would be surprised if people weren't more anxious about it. I do think it's important because the change is happening so fast, you need people to, in democracies, you need citizens to be engaged, be aware that this is happening, and make their preferences known."

— Sundar Pichai

"There is inevitable progress towards AGI that's happening, I've long understood it... The rate of progress over the last 1 to 2 years has made me feel it's on the closer side than not."

— Sundar Pichai
