SpaceX’s $2T Case, Nvidia’s Shock Selloff, America Turns on AI, Trump Pulls AI Order, Bond Crisis?

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·102:00·211.9K views·2026-05-22
Gavin BakerManaging Partner & CIO, Atreides Management Chamath PalihapitiyaCo-host, All-In Podcast Jason CalacanisCo-host, All-In Podcast
TL;DRSpaceX’s massive S-1 filing outlines a path to a $2 trillion valuation driven by Starlink’s high-margin profitability and a blockbuster $15 billion-per-year compute rental deal with Anthropic, even as Nvidia posts historic earnings and the broader…
AI sector faces a...

TL;DR

SpaceX’s massive S-1 filing outlines a path to a $2 trillion valuation driven by Starlink’s high-margin profitability and a blockbuster $15 billion-per-year compute rental deal with Anthropic, even as Nvidia posts historic earnings and the broader AI sector faces a growing public relations and macroeconomic backlash.

SpaceX S-1 Teardown: The Case for a $2T Valuation

  • The Landmark Public Offering: SpaceX has filed its S-1 to raise $75 billion at a $1.75 trillion valuation (under ticker SPCX), with an expected listing date of June 12.
  • Segment Financial Breakdown:
      • Starlink (The Cash Cow): Generated $11.4 billion in revenue last year (50% YoY growth) with $4.4 billion in operating income, serving over 10 million subscribers.
      • Space Launch Business: Recorded $4 billion in revenue (17% growth) but posted a $650 million operating loss.
      • AI Segment (xAI / Compute): Generated $3.2 billion in revenue but suffered a $6.4 billion operating loss, heavily impacted by a massive $20 billion capex cycle (over 60% of which went to AI compute buildout).
  • "Elon Web Services" (EWS) Explodes:
      • The Anthropic Deal: In a massive validation of SpaceX's ground-based infrastructure, Anthropic is renting out the "Colossus 1" and "Colossus 2" supercomputing clusters for $1.25 billion per month ($15 billion annually, or $45 billion over three years). Either party can terminate the deal with 90 days' notice.
      • Infrastructural Dominance: Chamath Palihapitiya highlighted that SpaceX's ability to quickly deliver a 1-gigawatt data center provides an unmatched competitive moat.
  • Orbital Compute and Starship's Multiplier:
      • Rapid Reusability: Starship is designed for rapid reusability (flying multiple times a day), which will dwarf the rest of the world's cumulative orbital payload capacity.
      • Space-Based Data Centers: Gavin Baker predicts that commercial orbital compute will become a reality between 2028 and 2030, noting that a working, non-radiation-hardened Nvidia H100 GPU is already operational in space today.

Nvidia’s Historic Earnings & The Semiconductor Horizon

  • Mind-Boggling Financial Performance: Nvidia reported Q1 revenue of $81.6 billion (up 85% YoY and 20% QoQ), translating to $58 billion in net income and $48 billion in free cash flow at a 75% gross margin.
  • Unprecedented Capital Returns: Nvidia announced an additional $80 billion in share buybacks and raised its quarterly dividend 25x (to 25 cents per share), pledging to return 50% of free cash flow to shareholders.
  • The $20B CPU Surprise: Nvidia’s CPU segment is projected to reach $20 billion in revenue this year, making it one of the largest CPU businesses in the world overnight.
  • Debunking the GPU Lifespan Bear Case:
      • Extended Hardware Utility: Gavin Baker argued that the bear case warning of short 2-year GPU lifespans is wrong. By offloading decode tasks to domain-specific architectures (such as Cerebras or Groq), the useful lifespan of older Nvidia GPUs can be extended to 10–15 years.
      • Asset-Backed Financing: This extended hardware utility allows companies like CoreWeave to secure cheap asset-backed financing at rates as low as 6% to 7%.

AI Development: Karpathy’s Move, Recursive Improvement, and the PR Crisis

  • Andrej Karpathy Joins Anthropic: The 39-year-old AI pioneer will lead Anthropic’s pre-training team with a focus on "recursive self-improvement" (models training models). This architectural approach could drive 10x annual performance gains.
  • The AI Public Relations Crisis:
      • Anti-Humanist Sentiments: Commencement speakers are being booed over AI. David Friedberg noted that public resentment stems from power centralization, asymmetric wealth distribution, and a deep psychological threat to the human ego.
      • Foreign Influence: Friedberg also warned of possible state-sponsored (e.g., Chinese/CCP-backed) media campaigns designed to slow down American data center development.
  • "Dystopian" Corporate Layoffs:
      • Cloudflare’s Branding Blunder: CEO Matthew Prince was criticized for a public memo announcing the layoff of 20% of his workforce, specifically targeting middle-management "measurers" while posting record revenues.
      • Meta's Employee Tracking: Mark Zuckerberg faced internal backlash after deploying recording software on all employee computers to harvest coding data for Llama models, laying off engineers who had previously optimized their own workflows with AI.

Macroeconomic Headwinds and Geopolitical Realignment

  • Flashing Economic Red Signals:
      • Resurgent Inflation: Professional forecasters are predicting CPI could hit 4% to 6% in Q2, with Polymarket placing a 99% probability on May inflation exceeding 4.2%.
      • Sovereign Bond Yield Spikes: U.S. 10-year yields reached 4.6%, while Japan's 30-year yields hit an all-time high of 5.1%, threatening to trigger a global carry-trade credit crisis.
  • The U.S. Energy and Resource Shield: Gavin Baker emphasized that even if global logistics routes (like the Strait of Hormuz) close, the U.S. remains insulated. The country is entirely self-sufficient in food, cheap domestic natural gas, and is now the world's largest oil and gas exporter.
  • The Xi Jinping Tech Summit: A 48-hour meeting between U.S. tech executives, Donald Trump, and Xi Jinping yielded transactional agricultural and aviation sales but no grand de-escalation deal. Baker advocated for continuing the sale of lower-tier, deprecated Nvidia GPUs to China to disincentivize them from building a fully independent, highly competitive semiconductor ecosystem.

Notable Quotes

"What [Elon Musk] creates is a capital moat that then accelerates a technology moat that then accelerates an execution and a learning moat. And that flywheel, when it starts to spin very quickly... will look very cheap, I think, in a few years." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"There's something about AI that's very not human-centric... It's almost anti-humanist. And I think that that's a deep psychological current for a lot of people, and their disdain for this technology fuels it." — David Friedberg
"Who cares what Matthew Prince thinks? Because the reality is that if this is the way that you're going to message something as critical as this, I think you did a horrible job. And now you label these people and you put a scarlet letter on their back." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"Rates going up and inflation going up is never good. But we have to hold what's happening with AI where the fundamentals are getting a lot stronger in our mind." — Gavin Baker

When you hear young people booing AI vociferously, why are they doing that, Freeburg? And what's your take on the overall PR problem and how to turn it around? That's a... there's a long answer to that question. It relates in some ways to your concerns about socialism and polarization. Yeah. What's the long answer? I mean, why do people hate technology? Why do they hate this technology? They love their phones. They love the internet. This technology they hate. I think that there's like an underlying view that technology creates leverage for a small group of people, which creates power imbalances, and nothing represents that more than AI. A small number of people that control and profit from and benefit from AI are going to end up getting outsized returns relative to the broader population. That the time to diffusion of the technology—because ultimately all technologies are commoditized and diffused—but the time to diffusion here is such that it's going to be like extremely asymmetric for society. And I think that there is something fundamental about that. It's like, you know, nuclear bombs I think really created this moment in people's minds in the mid-20th century that by the back half of the 20th century gave everyone a high degree of skepticism about technology and science generally. That those who have the knowledge and those who engineer solutions with the knowledge can create outsized advantages for themselves, and it puts the rest of us at risk—the rest of the world, the rest of the population at risk. And because those questions about when does this benefit me, how does it benefit me can't be answered today, the economic benefit that's accruing to the few today becomes the narrative. It becomes the story and it becomes this like power system that a few people take from the many. And so there's something deeply disturbing for the average person about that. They don't understand how it works, why it works, what it'll do for them, when it will do it, and all that they're being told is that some people are making trillions of dollars. So, I think that it's pretty obvious why this has got such a backlash. Secondly, I think that there's a deep amount of external energy that's fueling this anti-technology sentiment in the United States and has been for decades. I think, to Gavin's point, I don't think it's just China with NGOs today. I think that there is a long history of state actors intervening in media activities in foreign nations to try and create the sentiment and fuel a sentiment that reduces progress in that competitive state. I think this goes all the way back to KGB design during the Cold War and it's been refined and honed and improved over time. This is not just some conspiracy theory. There are plenty of great books about this. The techniques of what's going on specifically today, I don't know enough. I don't have any great details on that. But I don't think that there's no foreign interest in seeing technology advancement slow in competitive nations. The United States probably does similar things to other nations, and I think that that's probably a key part of this. And then I think this third piece is like when the Copernican revolution happened. Heliocentricity was a totally new way of thinking for humans, and it was deeply disruptive to the church. And it was deeply disruptive to the power centers, which were the centers that could tell people, "Earth is at the center of the universe, we're in control, we're the direct channel to God." And the idea that the sun is at the center of the solar system and we spin around it and we're a tiny speck in the universe was very hard for people to grasp. There's something about AI that's very like not human-centric, and it kind of shifts and messes with the ego of the human. It's almost anti-humanist. And I think that that's like a deep psychological current in a lot of people, and their disdain for this technology fuels it. It's not the cause, but I think it fuels it. So I think there's a lot of complicated aspects to this, J-Cal. You know, I don't think there's like a simple "put Chamath on a podcast and he'll solve the problems of AI right now." I think that there's a real set of shifts happening, and there's a real set of global competition underway where, you know, various state actors and interests are competing with each other. Yeah, Chamath, do you think that we should slow down? I don't think you can. No, no, no. Do you think we should slow down? No, I think... I was just talking to some people on a Zoom right before this. But I think after the Manhattan Project, the research labs were stood up to maintain our scientists that worked on the Manhattan Project from effectively leaching back or leaking back to Russia and Germany and other places that were adversaries to the United States. And they all were against the nuclear bomb. They worked on it because it was necessary for the United States security, but then when Russia got a hold of the secrets, they were leaked because people were worried that if the US had all the power, there would be no counter-balance to the power. And so the nuclear secrets were leaked to Russia for that purpose. Then when Russia had the nuclear secrets and they began developing hydrogen fusion bombs, and it was clear that they were going to race ahead, the United States raced ahead with developing nuclear bombs as a counter-balance to Russia. When the proliferation began, there was no stopping it. It began and you had to have this balance in the world, otherwise you have effectively an asymmetric power that can do whatever it wants globally. I think there's that moment in the world right now where if the United States does not advance its AI technology—the availability of it, TBD, industry, taxation, all these things that we're talking about doing—there will be someone else that will. Chip design from the bottom up – Reiner Pope

Dwarkesh Patel(1.3M)·80:20·39.5K views·2026-05-22
Dwarkesh PatelHost Reiner PopeCEO, MatX
TL;DRModern AI chip design optimizes the physical trade-offs between compute and communication by baking nested matrix multiplication loops directly into hardware via systolic arrays and utilizing software-managed scratchpads rather than unpredictable hardware caches.
The...

TL;DR

Modern AI chip design optimizes the physical trade-offs between compute and communication by baking nested matrix multiplication loops directly into hardware via systolic arrays and utilizing software-managed scratchpads rather than unpredictable hardware caches.

The Mathematics and Physics of Multiply-Accumulate (MAC)

  • The Fundamental Primitive: AI chips fundamentally rely on Multiply-Accumulate (MAC) operations ($output = input\_A \times input\_B + accumulator$) because they form the core of the nested loops that define matrix multiplication.
      • Asymmetric Precision: AI chips utilize lower precision for multiplication (e.g., 4-bit) and higher precision for accumulation (e.g., 8-bit). This is because rounding errors compound rapidly over repeated addition steps, whereas multiplication happens only once per computational chain.
  • Gate Economics & Dadda Multipliers: Implementing multiplication at the silicon level requires balancing logic gates and physical wire layouts.
      • AND Gates for Partial Products: Generating all intermediate partial products for a $p$-bit by $q$-bit multiplication requires exactly $p \times q$ AND gates.
      • Full Adders (3-to-2 Compressors): The bulk of physical chip area is consumed by "full adders." A full adder takes three single-bit inputs and compresses them into a two-bit binary output.
      • The Dadda Multiplier: To minimize physical die area, chips use Dadda multipliers to continually apply full adders to columns of bits. A $p \times q$ multiplication accumulated into a $(p+q)$-bit number requires exactly $p \times q$ full adders, providing an elegant, highly predictable algebraic scaling law for hardware designers.

Systolic Arrays: Overcoming the Communication Tax

  • The Register Bottleneck: Prior to Nvidia's Volta GPU generation (which introduced Tensor Cores), traditional CUDA cores spent roughly 7/8ths of their physical circuit area on data movement—specifically reading and writing register files—rather than on the arithmetic logic units (ALUs) doing the actual math.
  • Systolic Array Architecture: To solve this "communication tax," hardware designers baked entire nested matrix-multiplication loops directly into the silicon.
      • Quadratic Scaling: By mapping a matrix-vector multiplication spatially across a grid of ALUs, compute scales quadratically ($x \times y$) while the register communication overhead remains linear ($x$).
      • Weight Stationary Design: In AI workloads, the weights of a neural network remain fixed for many cycles. Systolic arrays exploit this by storing weight matrices locally in registers directly adjacent to the ALUs, eliminating the need to repeatedly fetch them from the main register file.
      • Trickle Feeding: Because routing wires across a chip consumes valuable silicon real estate, weight matrices are "trickled" into the array slowly over multiple clock cycles (e.g., shifting rows down one by one) to minimize wire-routing bandwidth.

Synchronization, Pipeline Registers, and Clock Cycles

  • The Need for Synchronization: Modern chips feature over 100 billion transistors. Because of microscopic manufacturing variations, signals travel at slightly different speeds across different paths of the die. To prevent data from arriving out of order and causing logical errors, the entire chip pauses and synchronizes in lockstep at every clock cycle (typically every nanosecond).
  • Pipeline Register Insertion: Chip architects manually and automatically insert "pipeline registers" to split long, continuous paths of logic into smaller segments.
      • Throughput vs. Area Trade-off: Splitting a logic path in half allows the clock speed to double (e.g., from 1 GHz to 2 GHz). However, registers consume significant die area (roughly 8x the size of a simple AND gate). If clock speeds are pushed too high (e.g., 5–6 GHz), registers consume nearly the entire chip, decreasing overall area-adjusted throughput.
      • Feedback Loop Constraints: Pipeline registers cannot be easily inserted into feedback loops (such as accumulation registers that add to themselves). Splitting a feedback loop with a register changes the fundamental math, resulting in two interleaved running sums (e.g., one for even cycles, one for odd) instead of one continuous sum.

Architectural Philosophies: FPGAs, CPUs, and TPUs

  • FPGAs vs. ASICs: Field Programmable Gate Arrays (FPGAs) emulate custom silicon on a fixed physical die using Lookup Tables (LUTs) and configurable multiplexers (muxes).
      • The 10x Overhead: FPGAs are roughly an order of magnitude more expensive and less energy-efficient than Application-Specific Integrated Circuits (ASICs). Emulating a simple 3-gate AND circuit inside an FPGA's programmable LUT requires a 16-input multiplexer consisting of roughly 32 physical gates.
      • The Business Trade-off: While FPGAs are inefficient, they can be reprogrammed in the "field" instantly. This allows companies to bypass the $30 million tape-out costs of an ASIC when deploying highly customized, low-latency code (e.g., in high-frequency trading).
  • Caches vs. Scratchpads:
      • Non-Deterministic CPU Caches: CPUs rely on hardware-managed caches to dynamically store recently accessed memory. Because cache hits depend on unpredictable environmental factors (such as background tasks or random cache-eviction policies), CPU latency is inherently non-deterministic.
      • Deterministic TPU Scratchpads: AI accelerators like Google TPUs achieve perfectly deterministic latency by replacing caches with "scratchpad" memories. Instead of relying on hardware-controlled guessing, the software compiler explicitly schedules every memory transfer between High Bandwidth Memory (HBM) and the local scratchpad.
  • GPU vs. TPU Block Layouts:
      • GPUs (Fine-Grained): Consist of a highly regular grid of many small Streaming Multiprocessors (SMs), each containing small Tensor Cores, registers, and individual warp schedulers.
      • TPUs (Coarse-Grained): Consist of a few massive, monolithic Matrix Multiply Units (MXUs) clustered around a centralized vector unit, amortizing scheduling and instruction-decoding overhead over massive matrix operations. MatX is developing a "splittable systolic array" designed to bridge these two paradigms.

You can find these open positions and watch the full interview at janestreet.com/dwarkesh. Where does the clock cycle of a chip come in? What determines what that is? Yeah. And what is a clock cycle of a chip? So, I guess at baseline, it's sort of worth observing that chips are incredibly, incredibly parallel, right? You've got 100 billion transistors in a chip. A key thing that you need to do whenever you have like massive parallelism is you need to synchronize between the different parallel units. In software, typically, you have these very expensive synchronization methods like a mutex, so one thread will finish what it's doing, it will grab a lock somewhere stored in memory and then notify the other thread that it's done. On chips, we take a very different approach and say that every like nanosecond or so, all circuitry in the chip will kind of pause for a moment and then synchronize. So, it actually synchronizes every single nanosecond or so. And so, that is the clock cycle. The entire chip, typically, all in sort of one fell swoop goes in lockstep to the next operation that happens. And so, what this looks like in circuitry is that you will have... This is typically drawn... The clock is sort of mediated by registers, which are these storage devices that we've drawn elsewhere. And the way to think of it is that I have some storage, which is storing like a bit, which might be 0 or 1, and then I have some sort of cloud of logic, which maybe is like this systolic array, or this multiplier, or something like that, and then that's going to produce some output. So, my inputs... I've got a bunch of inputs feeding into this cloud of logic, and then eventually later there's going to be some output register that this writes to. There is a global clock signal which drives all of these registers, and it says at a certain instance in time when the clock strikes, whatever value happens to be on this wire at that instant, that's what's going to get stored in there. And so, the challenge here is like, "I would like to have my clock speed run as fast as possible, because if I can run at 2 GHz, I can get twice as many operations done per second than if I run at 1 GHz." But what that ends up meaning is that I am very sensitive to the delay through this cloud of logic, because any computation that is going to happen in here needs to sort of finish before the next clock cycle hits. Right. So, a major point of sort of optimization on any chip then is to make this delay from here as short as possible. Interesting, and is there ever, because the constraint here seems to be that if you add too much logic, then you might risk missing the clock cycle, but if you don't add enough, then you're, you know, leaving potential compute on the table... Is there ever a situation where you'd take a probabilistic chance that a computation finishes, or is it just like, "No, either it's going to finish by clock cycle or not?" Yeah, in standard chip design you margin it such that... I mean, there is a probability, but it's like many, many standard deviations, like way standard deviations out, such that for all intents and purposes, it is a reliable part; it will always meet the clock. There are some weird exceptions to that, like clock domain crossings where you go from one clock to the other clock, and then you actually do have to reason about this probability, but in the main path, you just margin it such that you'll get there like 25% of the clock cycle in advance, so that it's very unlikely that it won't. And this clock where the clocks synchronize, I guess where the registers are, this is not something you'd determine as a chip designer. This is sort of just like an artifact of, "Hey, I want whatever sequence of logic," and then the software you use to convert your Verilog into the thing you send to TSMC, that just determines like, "Hey, in order to make this work, you've got to put a register here, here, and here, to make sure that there's no one step that is too long, such that it makes the whole clock cycle of the entire chip longer than it has to be." Yeah, so this is actually a huge part of the work of designing a chip, actually, is inserting them. So, it is done in a combination of manually and automatically. So, I mean, like just to show the very sort of dumb version of what you can do here: you can take this logic and split it in half, and so say, "Actually, instead of just one cloud of logic, I'm going to have two smaller clouds of logic, which do the same thing, but split them up by a register, like this." And this is like... If you split it in the middle, you can hit twice the clock frequency. That's great, you get twice the performance, at the cost of this extra register, and so at the cost of some more storage. And so, stepping back, why do we need to synchronize the whole chip? Like, if you imagine Factorio or something, there's no global clock cycle; it's just shit is done when it's done. There's iron on the plate, you can take it if you want. Yeah, so taking that analogy, the thing that you need to be mindful of is if I've got two different paths through some logic. Yeah, so, I mean, first, let's consider the business case for an FPGA versus an ASIC. FPGAs and ASICs use largely the same sort of conceptual model, which is that I have a series of gates built from ANDs, ORs, XORs—those like very small primitives—connected together with a fixed clock cycle, and connected together with wires that run in a fixed clock cycle. So, anything you can express in an FPGA, you can express in an ASIC, too, and it'll be about an order of magnitude cheaper and better energy efficiency on an ASIC than an FPGA. The trade-off is that the first FPGA costs you $10,000, whereas the first ASIC you make costs you $30 million, because it requires an entire tape out. So, sort of the business use case for an FPGA would be that, "I want something that has this very deterministic latency, and fast runtime, and high parallelism, but then, you know, I'm going to change it very frequently, change what I do every month or something like that, and so I don't want to pay the tape out cost every time." So, in a CPU you have the CPU die itself, and then there is a memory off on the side. This is the DDR memory off on the side, and then you have a cache system here inside it. The cache sort of remembers recent accesses to DDR and stores them. And so, when I'm running through my CPU instructions, every time I have an instruction that accesses memory, it first checks in the cache: "Was the data stored in the cache?" And then, if not, it goes and fetches out to DDR. Yep. This is a huge optimization. The cache is like two orders of magnitude faster than the DDR. If you never used the cache, basically all programs would run 100 times slower. So, the presence of a cache is absolutely necessary for a CPU to run at reasonable speed. But, whether or not you get a cache hit is dependent on the sort of ambient environment of the CPU, like, what other programs are running, what has run recently, and what is the random number generator inside the cache system doing.
